1727 "PREFACE TO FIRST EDITION"
1727 ""
1727 ""
1727 "This translation is intended to supplement a work entitled “The"
1727 "Authoress of the Odyssey”, which I published in 1897. I could not give"
1727 "the whole “Odyssey” in that book without making it unwieldy, I"
1727 "therefore epitomised my translation, which was already completed and"
1727 "which I now publish in full."
1727 ""
1727 "I shall not here argue the two main points dealt with in the work just"
1727 "mentioned; I have nothing either to add to, or to withdraw from, what I"
1727 "have there written. The points in question are:"
1727 ""
1727 "(1) that the “Odyssey” was written entirely at, and drawn entirely"
1727 "from, the place now called Trapani on the West Coast of Sicily, alike"
1727 "as regards the Phaeacian and the Ithaca scenes; while the voyages of"
1727 "Ulysses, when once he is within easy reach of Sicily, solve themselves"
1727 "into a periplus of the island, practically from Trapani back to"
1727 "Trapani, via the Lipari islands, the Straits of Messina, and the island"
1727 "of Pantellaria."
1727 ""
1727 "(2) That the poem was entirely written by a very young woman, who lived"
1727 "at the place now called Trapani, and introduced herself into her work"
1727 "under the name of Nausicaa."
1727 ""
1727 "The main arguments on which I base the first of these somewhat"
1727 "startling contentions, have been prominently and repeatedly before the"
1727 "English and Italian public ever since they appeared (without rejoinder)"
1727 "in the “Athenaeum” for January 30 and February 20, 1892. Both"
1727 "contentions were urged (also without rejoinder) in the Johnian “Eagle”"
1727 "for the Lent and October terms of the same year. Nothing to which I"
1727 "should reply has reached me from any quarter, and knowing how anxiously"
1727 "I have endeavoured to learn the existence of any flaws in my argument,"
1727 "I begin to feel some confidence that, did such flaws exist, I should"
1727 "have heard, at any rate about some of them, before now. Without,"
1727 "therefore, for a moment pretending to think that scholars generally"
1727 "acquiesce in my conclusions, I shall act as thinking them little likely"
1727 "so to gainsay me as that it will be incumbent upon me to reply, and"
1727 "shall confine myself to translating the “Odyssey” for English readers,"
1727 "with such notes as I think will be found useful. Among these I would"
1727 "especially call attention to one on xxii. 465-473 which Lord Grimthorpe"
1727 "has kindly allowed me to make public."
1727 ""
1727 "I have repeated several of the illustrations used in “The Authoress of"
1727 "the Odyssey”, and have added two which I hope may bring the outer court"
1727 "of Ulysses’ house more vividly before the reader. I should like to"
1727 "explain that the presence of a man and a dog in one illustration is"
1727 "accidental, and was not observed by me till I developed the negative."
1727 "In an appendix I have also reprinted the paragraphs explanatory of the"
1727 "plan of Ulysses’ house, together with the plan itself. The reader is"
1727 "recommended to study this plan with some attention."
1727 ""
1727 "In the preface to my translation of the “Iliad” I have given my views"
1727 "as to the main principles by which a translator should be guided, and"
1727 "need not repeat them here, beyond pointing out that the initial liberty"
1727 "of translating poetry into prose involves the continual taking of more"
1727 "or less liberty throughout the translation; for much that is right in"
1727 "poetry is wrong in prose, and the exigencies of readable prose are the"
1727 "first things to be considered in a prose translation. That the reader,"
1727 "however, may see how far I have departed from strict construe, I will"
1727 "print here Messrs. Butcher and Lang’s translation of the sixty lines or"
1727 "so of the “Odyssey.” Their translation runs:"
1727 ""
1727 "Tell me, Muse, of that man, so ready at need, who wandered far and"
1727 "wide, after he had sacked the sacred citadel of Troy, and many were the"
1727 "men whose towns he saw and whose mind he learnt, yea, and many the woes"
1727 "he suffered in his heart on the deep, striving to win his own life and"
1727 "the return of his company. Nay, but even so he saved not his company,"
1727 "though he desired it sore. For through the blindness of their own"
1727 "hearts they perished, fools, who devoured the oxen of Helios Hyperion:"
1727 "but the god took from them their day of returning. Of these things,"
1727 "goddess, daughter of Zeus, whencesoever thou hast heard thereof,"
1727 "declare thou even unto us."
1727 ""
1727 "    Now all the rest, as many as fled from sheer destruction, were at"
1727 "    home, and had escaped both war and sea, but Odysseus only, craving"
1727 "    for his wife and for his homeward path, the lady nymph Calypso"
1727 "    held, that fair goddess, in her hollow caves, longing to have him"
1727 "    for her lord. But when now the year had come in the courses of the"
1727 "    seasons, wherein the gods had ordained that he should return home"
1727 "    to Ithaca, not even there was he quit of labours, not even among"
1727 "    his own; but all the gods had pity on him save Poseidon, who raged"
1727 "    continually against godlike Odysseus, till he came to his own"
1727 "    country. Howbeit Poseidon had now departed for the distant"
1727 "    Ethiopians, the Ethiopians that are sundered in twain, the"
1727 "    uttermost of men, abiding some where Hyperion sinks and some where"
1727 "    he rises. There he looked to receive his hecatomb of bulls and"
1727 "    rams, there he made merry sitting at the feast, but the other gods"
1727 "    were gathered in the halls of Olympian Zeus. Then among them the"
1727 "    father of men and gods began to speak, for he bethought him in his"
1727 "    heart of noble Aegisthus, whom the son of Agamemnon, far-famed"
1727 "    Orestes, slew. Thinking upon him he spake out among the Immortals:"
1727 "    ‘Lo you now, how vainly mortal men do blame the gods! For of us"
1727 "    they say comes evil, whereas they even of themselves, through the"
1727 "    blindness of their own hearts, have sorrows beyond that which is"
1727 "    ordained. Even as of late Aegisthus, beyond that which was"
1727 "    ordained, took to him the wedded wife of the son of Atreus, and"
1727 "    killed her lord on his return, and that with sheer doom before his"
1727 "    eyes, since we had warned him by the embassy of Hermes the"
1727 "    keen-sighted, the slayer of Argos, that he should neither kill the"
1727 "    man, nor woo his wife. For the son of Atreus shall be avenged at"
1727 "    the hand of Orestes, so soon as he shall come to man’s estate and"
1727 "    long for his own country. So spake Hermes, yet he prevailed not on"
1727 "    the heart of Aegisthus, for all his good will; but now hath he paid"
1727 "    one price for all.’"
1727 ""
1727 "    And the goddess, grey-eyed Athene, answered him, saying: ‘O father,"
1727 "    our father Cronides, throned in the highest; that man assuredly"
1727 "    lies in a death that is his due; so perish likewise all who work"
1727 "    such deeds! But my heart is rent for wise Odysseus, the hapless"
1727 "    one, who far from his friends this long while suffereth affliction"
1727 "    in a sea-girt isle, where is the navel of the sea, a woodland isle,"
1727 "    and therein a goddess hath her habitation, the daughter of the"
1727 "    wizard Atlas, who knows the depths of every sea, and himself"
1727 "    upholds the tall pillars which keep earth and sky asunder. His"
1727 "    daughter it is that holds the hapless man in sorrow: and ever with"
1727 "    soft and guileful tales she is wooing him to forgetfulness of"
1727 "    Ithaca. But Odysseus yearning to see if it were but the smoke leap"
1727 "    upwards from his own land, hath a desire to die. As for thee, thine"
1727 "    heart regardeth it not at all, Olympian! What! Did not Odysseus by"
1727 "    the ships of the Argives make thee free offering of sacrifice in"
1727 "    the wide Trojan land? Wherefore wast thou then so wroth with him, O"
1727 "    Zeus?’"
1727 ""
1727 ""
1727 "The “Odyssey” (as every one knows) abounds in passages borrowed from"
1727 "the “Iliad”; I had wished to print these in a slightly different type,"
1727 "with marginal references to the “Iliad,” and had marked them to this"
1727 "end in my MS. I found, however, that the translation would be thus"
1727 "hopelessly scholasticised, and abandoned my intention. I would"
1727 "nevertheless urge on those who have the management of our University"
1727 "presses, that they would render a great service to students if they"
1727 "would publish a Greek text of the “Odyssey” with the Iliadic passages"
1727 "printed in a different type, and with marginal references. I have given"
1727 "the British Museum a copy of the “Odyssey” with the Iliadic passages"
1727 "underlined and referred to in MS.; I have also given an “Iliad” marked"
1727 "with all the Odyssean passages, and their references; but copies of"
1727 "both the “Iliad” and “Odyssey” so marked ought to be within easy reach"
1727 "of all students."
1727 ""
1727 "Any one who at the present day discusses the questions that have arisen"
1727 "round the “Iliad” since Wolf’s time, without keeping it well before his"
1727 "reader’s mind that the “Odyssey” was demonstrably written from one"
1727 "single neighbourhood, and hence (even though nothing else pointed to"
1727 "this conclusion) presumably by one person only—that it was written"
1727 "certainly before 750, and in all probability before 1000 B.C.—that the"
1727 "writer of this very early poem was demonstrably familiar with the"
1727 "“Iliad” as we now have it, borrowing as freely from those books whose"
1727 "genuineness has been most impugned, as from those which are admitted to"
1727 "be by Homer—any one who fails to keep these points before his readers,"
1727 "is hardly dealing equitably by them. Any one on the other hand, who"
1727 "will mark his “Iliad” and his “Odyssey” from the copies in the British"
1727 "Museum above referred to, and who will draw the only inference that"
1727 "common sense can draw from the presence of so many identical passages"
1727 "in both poems, will, I believe, find no difficulty in assigning their"
1727 "proper value to a large number of books here and on the Continent that"
1727 "at present enjoy considerable reputations. Furthermore, and this"
1727 "perhaps is an advantage better worth securing, he will find that many"
1727 "puzzles of the “Odyssey” cease to puzzle him on the discovery that they"
1727 "arise from over-saturation with the “Iliad.”"
1727 ""
1727 "Other difficulties will also disappear as soon as the development of"
1727 "the poem in the writer’s mind is understood. I have dealt with this at"
1727 "some length in pp. 251-261 of “The Authoress of the Odyssey”. Briefly,"
1727 "the “Odyssey” consists of two distinct poems: (1) The Return of"
1727 "Ulysses, which alone the Muse is asked to sing in the opening lines of"
1727 "the poem. This poem includes the Phaeacian episode, and the account of"
1727 "Ulysses’ adventures as told by himself in Books ix.-xii. It consists of"
1727 "lines 1-79 (roughly) of Book i., of line 28 of Book v., and thence"
1727 "without intermission to the middle of line 187 of Book xiii., at which"
1727 "point the original scheme was abandoned."
1727 ""
1727 "(2) The story of Penelope and the suitors, with the episode of"
1727 "Telemachus’ voyage to Pylos. This poem begins with line 80 (roughly) of"
1727 "Book i., is continued to the end of Book iv., and not resumed till"
1727 "Ulysses wakes in the middle of line 187, Book xiii., from whence it"
1727 "continues to the end of Book xxiv."
1727 ""
1727 "In “The Authoress of the Odyssey”, I wrote:"
1727 ""
1727 "the introduction of lines xi., 115-137 and of line ix., 535, with the"
1727 "writing a new council of the gods at the beginning of Book v., to take"
1727 "the place of the one that was removed to Book i., 1-79, were the only"
1727 "things that were done to give even a semblance of unity to the old"
1727 "scheme and the new, and to conceal the fact that the Muse, after being"
1727 "asked to sing of one subject, spend two-thirds of her time in singing a"
1727 "very different one, with a climax for which no-one has asked her. For"
1727 "roughly the Return occupies eight Books, and Penelope and the Suitors"
1727 "sixteen."
1727 ""
1727 ""
1727 "I believe this to be substantially correct."
1727 ""
1727 "Lastly, to deal with a very unimportant point, I observe that the"
1727 "Leipsic Teubner edition of 894 makes Books ii. and iii. end with a"
1727 "comma. Stops are things of such far more recent date than the"
1727 "“Odyssey,” that there does not seem much use in adhering to the text in"
1727 "so small a matter; still, from a spirit of mere conservatism, I have"
1727 "preferred to do so. Why [Greek] at the beginnings of Books ii. and"
1727 "viii., and [Greek], at the beginning of Book vii. should have initial"
1727 "capitals in an edition far too careful to admit a supposition of"
1727 "inadvertence, when [Greek] at the beginning of Books vi. and xiii., and"
1727 "[Greek] at the beginning of Book xvii. have no initial capitals, I"
1727 "cannot determine. No other Books of the “Odyssey” have initial capitals"
1727 "except the three mentioned unless the first word of the Book is a"
1727 "proper name."

1727 "PREFACE TO SECOND EDITION"
1727 ""
1727 ""
1727 "Butler’s Translation of the “Odyssey” appeared originally in 1900, and"
1727 "The Authoress of the Odyssey in 1897. In the preface to the new edition"
1727 "of “The Authoress”, which is published simultaneously with this new"
1727 "edition of the Translation, I have given some account of the genesis of"
1727 "the two books."
1727 ""
1727 "The size of the original page has been reduced so as to make both books"
1727 "uniform with Butler’s other works; and, fortunately, it has been"
1727 "possible, by using a smaller type, to get the same number of words into"
1727 "each page, so that the references remain good, and, with the exception"
1727 "of a few minor alterations and rearrangements now to be enumerated so"
1727 "far as they affect the Translation, the new editions are faithful"
1727 "reprints of the original editions, with misprints and obvious errors"
1727 "corrected—no attempt having been made to edit them or to bring them up"
1727 "to date."
1727 ""
1727 "(a) The Index has been revised."
1727 ""
1727 "(b) Owing to the reduction in the size of the page it has been"
1727 "necessary to shorten some of the headlines, and here advantage has been"
1727 "taken of various corrections of and additions to the headlines and"
1727 "shoulder-notes made by Butler in his own copies of the two books."
1727 ""
1727 "(c) For the most part each of the illustrations now occupies a page,"
1727 "whereas in the original editions they generally appeared two on the"
1727 "page. It has been necessary to reduce the plan of the House of Ulysses."
1727 ""
1727 "On page 153 of “The Authoress” Butler says: “No great poet would"
1727 "compare his hero to a paunch full of blood and fat, cooking before the"
1727 "fire (xx, 24-28).” This passage is not given in the abridged Story of"
1727 "the “Odyssey” at the beginning of the book, but in the Translation it"
1727 "occurs in these words:"
1727 ""
1727 "“Thus he chided with his heart, and checked it into endurance, but he"
1727 "tossed about as one who turns a paunch full of blood and fat in front"
1727 "of a hot fire, doing it first on one side then on the other, that he"
1727 "may get it cooked as soon as possible; even so did he turn himself"
1727 "about from side to side, thinking all the time how, single-handed as he"
1727 "was, he should contrive to kill so large a body of men as the wicked"
1727 "suitors.”"
1727 ""
1727 "It looks as though in the interval between the publication of “The"
1727 "Authoress” (1897) and of the Translation (1900) Butler had changed his"
1727 "mind; for in the first case the comparison is between Ulysses and a"
1727 "paunch full, etc., and in the second it is between Ulysses and a man"
1727 "who turns a paunch full, etc. The second comparison is perhaps one"
1727 "which a great poet might make."
1727 ""
1727 "In seeing the works through the press I have had the invaluable"
1727 "assistance of Mr. A. T. Bartholomew of the University Library,"
1727 "Cambridge, and of Mr. Donald S. Robertson, Fellow of Trinity College,"
1727 "Cambridge. To both these friends I give my most cordial thanks for the"
1727 "care and skill exercised by them. Mr. Robertson has found time for the"
1727 "labour of checking and correcting all the quotations from and"
1727 "references to the “Iliad” and “Odyssey,” and I believe that it could"
1727 "not have been better performed. It was, I know, a pleasure for him; and"
1727 "it would have been a pleasure also for Butler if he could have known"
1727 "that his work was being shepherded by the son of his old friend, Mr. H."
1727 "R. Robertson, who more than half a century ago was a fellow-student"
1727 "with him at Cary’s School of Art in Streatham Street, Bloomsbury."

1727 ""
1727 "THE GODS IN COUNCIL—MINERVA’S VISIT TO ITHACA—THE CHALLENGE FROM"
1727 "TELEMACHUS TO THE SUITORS."
1727 ""
1727 ""
1727 "Tell me, O Muse, of that ingenious hero who travelled far and wide"
1727 "after he had sacked the famous town of Troy. Many cities did he visit,"
1727 "and many were the nations with whose manners and customs he was"
1727 "acquainted; moreover he suffered much by sea while trying to save his"
1727 "own life and bring his men safely home; but do what he might he could"
1727 "not save his men, for they perished through their own sheer folly in"
1727 "eating the cattle of the Sun-god Hyperion; so the god prevented them"
1727 "from ever reaching home. Tell me, too, about all these things, oh"
1727 "daughter of Jove, from whatsoever source you may know them."
1727 ""
1727 "So now all who escaped death in battle or by shipwreck had got safely"
1727 "home except Ulysses, and he, though he was longing to return to his"
1727 "wife and country, was detained by the goddess Calypso, who had got him"
1727 "into a large cave and wanted to marry him. But as years went by, there"
1727 "came a time when the gods settled that he should go back to Ithaca;"
1727 "even then, however, when he was among his own people, his troubles were"
1727 "not yet over; nevertheless all the gods had now begun to pity him"
1727 "except Neptune, who still persecuted him without ceasing and would not"
1727 "let him get home."
1727 ""
1727 "Now Neptune had gone off to the Ethiopians, who are at the world’s end,"
1727 "and lie in two halves, the one looking West and the other East.1 He had"
1727 "gone there to accept a hecatomb of sheep and oxen, and was enjoying"
1727 "himself at his festival; but the other gods met in the house of"
1727 "Olympian Jove, and the sire of gods and men spoke first. At that moment"
1727 "he was thinking of Aegisthus, who had been killed by Agamemnon’s son"
1727 "Orestes; so he said to the other gods:"
1727 ""
1727 "“See now, how men lay blame upon us gods for what is after all nothing"
1727 "but their own folly. Look at Aegisthus; he must needs make love to"
1727 "Agamemnon’s wife unrighteously and then kill Agamemnon, though he knew"
1727 "it would be the death of him; for I sent Mercury to warn him not to do"
1727 "either of these things, inasmuch as Orestes would be sure to take his"
1727 "revenge when he grew up and wanted to return home. Mercury told him"
1727 "this in all good will but he would not listen, and now he has paid for"
1727 "everything in full.”"
1727 ""
1727 "Then Minerva said, “Father, son of Saturn, King of kings, it served"
1727 "Aegisthus right, and so it would any one else who does as he did; but"
1727 "Aegisthus is neither here nor there; it is for Ulysses that my heart"
1727 "bleeds, when I think of his sufferings in that lonely sea-girt island,"
1727 "far away, poor man, from all his friends. It is an island covered with"
1727 "forest, in the very middle of the sea, and a goddess lives there,"
1727 "daughter of the magician Atlas, who looks after the bottom of the"
1727 "ocean, and carries the great columns that keep heaven and earth"
1727 "asunder. This daughter of Atlas has got hold of poor unhappy Ulysses,"
1727 "and keeps trying by every kind of blandishment to make him forget his"
1727 "home, so that he is tired of life, and thinks of nothing but how he may"
1727 "once more see the smoke of his own chimneys. You, sir, take no heed of"
1727 "this, and yet when Ulysses was before Troy did he not propitiate you"
1727 "with many a burnt sacrifice? Why then should you keep on being so angry"
1727 "with him?”"
1727 ""
1727 "And Jove said, “My child, what are you talking about? How can I forget"
1727 "Ulysses than whom there is no more capable man on earth, nor more"
1727 "liberal in his offerings to the immortal gods that live in heaven? Bear"
1727 "in mind, however, that Neptune is still furious with Ulysses for having"
1727 "blinded an eye of Polyphemus king of the Cyclopes. Polyphemus is son to"
1727 "Neptune by the nymph Thoosa, daughter to the sea-king Phorcys;"
1727 "therefore though he will not kill Ulysses outright, he torments him by"
1727 "preventing him from getting home. Still, let us lay our heads together"
1727 "and see how we can help him to return; Neptune will then be pacified,"
1727 "for if we are all of a mind he can hardly stand out against us.”"
1727 ""
1727 "And Minerva said, “Father, son of Saturn, King of kings, if, then, the"
1727 "gods now mean that Ulysses should get home, we should first send"
1727 "Mercury to the Ogygian island to tell Calypso that we have made up our"
1727 "minds and that he is to return. In the meantime I will go to Ithaca, to"
1727 "put heart into Ulysses’ son Telemachus; I will embolden him to call the"
1727 "Achaeans in assembly, and speak out to the suitors of his mother"
1727 "Penelope, who persist in eating up any number of his sheep and oxen; I"
1727 "will also conduct him to Sparta and to Pylos, to see if he can hear"
1727 "anything about the return of his dear father—for this will make people"
1727 "speak well of him.”"
1727 ""
1727 "So saying she bound on her glittering golden sandals, imperishable,"
1727 "with which she can fly like the wind over land or sea; she grasped the"
1727 "redoubtable bronze-shod spear, so stout and sturdy and strong,"
1727 "wherewith she quells the ranks of heroes who have displeased her, and"
1727 "down she darted from the topmost summits of Olympus, whereon forthwith"
1727 "she was in Ithaca, at the gateway of Ulysses’ house, disguised as a"
1727 "visitor, Mentes, chief of the Taphians, and she held a bronze spear in"
1727 "her hand. There she found the lordly suitors seated on hides of the"
1727 "oxen which they had killed and eaten, and playing draughts in front of"
1727 "the house. Men-servants and pages were bustling about to wait upon"
1727 "them, some mixing wine with water in the mixing-bowls, some cleaning"
1727 "down the tables with wet sponges and laying them out again, and some"
1727 "cutting up great quantities of meat."
1727 ""
1727 "Telemachus saw her long before any one else did. He was sitting moodily"
1727 "among the suitors thinking about his brave father, and how he would"
1727 "send them flying out of the house, if he were to come to his own again"
1727 "and be honoured as in days gone by. Thus brooding as he sat among them,"
1727 "he caught sight of Minerva and went straight to the gate, for he was"
1727 "vexed that a stranger should be kept waiting for admittance. He took"
1727 "her right hand in his own, and bade her give him her spear. “Welcome,”"
1727 "said he, “to our house, and when you have partaken of food you shall"
1727 "tell us what you have come for.”"
1727 ""
1727 "He led the way as he spoke, and Minerva followed him. When they were"
1727 "within he took her spear and set it in the spear-stand against a strong"
1727 "bearing-post along with the many other spears of his unhappy father,"
1727 "and he conducted her to a richly decorated seat under which he threw a"
1727 "cloth of damask. There was a footstool also for her feet,2 and he set"
1727 "another seat near her for himself, away from the suitors, that she"
1727 "might not be annoyed while eating by their noise and insolence, and"
1727 "that he might ask her more freely about his father."
1727 ""
1727 "A maid servant then brought them water in a beautiful golden ewer and"
1727 "poured it into a silver basin for them to wash their hands, and she"
1727 "drew a clean table beside them. An upper servant brought them bread,"

1727 "ASSEMBLY OF THE PEOPLE OF ITHACA—SPEECHES OF TELEMACHUS AND OF THE"
1727 "SUITORS—TELEMACHUS MAKES HIS PREPARATIONS AND STARTS FOR PYLOS WITH"
1727 "MINERVA DISGUISED AS MENTOR."
1727 ""
1727 ""
1727 "Now when the child of morning, rosy-fingered Dawn, appeared Telemachus"
1727 "rose and dressed himself. He bound his sandals on to his comely feet,"
1727 "girded his sword about his shoulder, and left his room looking like an"
1727 "immortal god. He at once sent the criers round to call the people in"
1727 "assembly, so they called them and the people gathered thereon; then,"
1727 "when they were got together, he went to the place of assembly spear in"
1727 "hand—not alone, for his two hounds went with him. Minerva endowed him"
1727 "with a presence of such divine comeliness that all marvelled at him as"
1727 "he went by, and when he took his place in his father’s seat even the"
1727 "oldest councillors made way for him."
1727 ""
1727 "Aegyptius, a man bent double with age, and of infinite experience, was"
1727 "the first to speak. His son Antiphus had gone with Ulysses to Ilius,"
1727 "land of noble steeds, but the savage Cyclops had killed him when they"
1727 "were all shut up in the cave, and had cooked his last dinner for him.17"
1727 "He had three sons left, of whom two still worked on their father’s"
1727 "land, while the third, Eurynomus, was one of the suitors; nevertheless"
1727 "their father could not get over the loss of Antiphus, and was still"
1727 "weeping for him when he began his speech."
1727 ""
1727 "“Men of Ithaca,” he said, “hear my words. From the day Ulysses left us"
1727 "there has been no meeting of our councillors until now; who then can it"
1727 "be, whether old or young, that finds it so necessary to convene us? Has"
1727 "he got wind of some host approaching, and does he wish to warn us, or"
1727 "would he speak upon some other matter of public moment? I am sure he is"
1727 "an excellent person, and I hope Jove will grant him his heart’s"
1727 "desire.”"
1727 ""
1727 "Telemachus took this speech as of good omen and rose at once, for he"
1727 "was bursting with what he had to say. He stood in the middle of the"
1727 "assembly and the good herald Pisenor brought him his staff. Then,"
1727 "turning to Aegyptius, “Sir,” said he, “it is I, as you will shortly"
1727 "learn, who have convened you, for it is I who am the most aggrieved. I"
1727 "have not got wind of any host approaching about which I would warn you,"
1727 "nor is there any matter of public moment on which I would speak. My"
1727 "grievance is purely personal, and turns on two great misfortunes which"
1727 "have fallen upon my house. The first of these is the loss of my"
1727 "excellent father, who was chief among all you here present, and was"
1727 "like a father to every one of you; the second is much more serious, and"
1727 "ere long will be the utter ruin of my estate. The sons of all the chief"
1727 "men among you are pestering my mother to marry them against her will."
1727 "They are afraid to go to her father Icarius, asking him to choose the"
1727 "one he likes best, and to provide marriage gifts for his daughter, but"
1727 "day by day they keep hanging about my father’s house, sacrificing our"
1727 "oxen, sheep, and fat goats for their banquets, and never giving so much"
1727 "as a thought to the quantity of wine they drink. No estate can stand"
1727 "such recklessness; we have now no Ulysses to ward off harm from our"
1727 "doors, and I cannot hold my own against them. I shall never all my days"
1727 "be as good a man as he was, still I would indeed defend myself if I had"
1727 "power to do so, for I cannot stand such treatment any longer; my house"
1727 "is being disgraced and ruined. Have respect, therefore, to your own"
1727 "consciences and to public opinion. Fear, too, the wrath of heaven, lest"
1727 "the gods should be displeased and turn upon you. I pray you by Jove and"
1727 "Themis, who is the beginning and the end of councils, [do not] hold"
1727 "back, my friends, and leave me singlehanded18—unless it be that my"
1727 "brave father Ulysses did some wrong to the Achaeans which you would now"
1727 "avenge on me, by aiding and abetting these suitors. Moreover, if I am"
1727 "to be eaten out of house and home at all, I had rather you did the"
1727 "eating yourselves, for I could then take action against you to some"
1727 "purpose, and serve you with notices from house to house till I got paid"
1727 "in full, whereas now I have no remedy.”19"
1727 ""
1727 "With this Telemachus dashed his staff to the ground and burst into"
1727 "tears. Every one was very sorry for him, but they all sat still and no"
1727 "one ventured to make him an angry answer, save only Antinous, who spoke"
1727 "thus:"
1727 ""
1727 "“Telemachus, insolent braggart that you are, how dare you try to throw"
1727 "the blame upon us suitors? It is your mother’s fault not ours, for she"
1727 "is a very artful woman. This three years past, and close on four, she"
1727 "had been driving us out of our minds, by encouraging each one of us,"
1727 "and sending him messages without meaning one word of what she says. And"
1727 "then there was that other trick she played us. She set up a great"
1727 "tambour frame in her room, and began to work on an enormous piece of"
1727 "fine needlework. ‘Sweet hearts,’ said she, ‘Ulysses is indeed dead,"
1727 "still do not press me to marry again immediately, wait—for I would not"
1727 "have skill in needlework perish unrecorded—till I have completed a pall"
1727 "for the hero Laertes, to be in readiness against the time when death"
1727 "shall take him. He is very rich, and the women of the place will talk"
1727 "if he is laid out without a pall.’"
1727 ""
1727 "“This was what she said, and we assented; whereon we could see her"
1727 "working on her great web all day long, but at night she would unpick"
1727 "the stitches again by torchlight. She fooled us in this way for three"
1727 "years and we never found her out, but as time wore on and she was now"
1727 "in her fourth year, one of her maids who knew what she was doing told"
1727 "us, and we caught her in the act of undoing her work, so she had to"
1727 "finish it whether she would or no. The suitors, therefore, make you"
1727 "this answer, that both you and the Achaeans may understand—‘Send your"
1727 "mother away, and bid her marry the man of her own and of her father’s"
1727 "choice’; for I do not know what will happen if she goes on plaguing us"
1727 "much longer with the airs she gives herself on the score of the"
1727 "accomplishments Minerva has taught her, and because she is so clever."
1727 "We never yet heard of such a woman; we know all about Tyro, Alcmena,"
1727 "Mycene, and the famous women of old, but they were nothing to your"
1727 "mother any one of them. It was not fair of her to treat us in that way,"
1727 "and as long as she continues in the mind with which heaven has now"
1727 "endowed her, so long shall we go on eating up your estate; and I do not"
1727 "see why she should change, for she gets all the honour and glory, and"
1727 "it is you who pay for it, not she. Understand, then, that we will not"
1727 "go back to our lands, neither here nor elsewhere, till she has made her"
1727 "choice and married some one or other of us.”"
1727 ""
1727 "Telemachus answered, “Antinous, how can I drive the mother who bore me"
1727 "from my father’s house? My father is abroad and we do not know whether"
1727 "he is alive or dead. It will be hard on me if I have to pay Icarius the"
1727 "large sum which I must give him if I insist on sending his daughter"
1727 "back to him. Not only will he deal rigorously with me, but heaven will"
1727 "also punish me; for my mother when she leaves the house will call on"
1727 "the Erinyes to avenge her; besides, it would not be a creditable thing"
1727 "to do, and I will have nothing to say to it. If you choose to take"
1727 "offence at this, leave the house and feast elsewhere at one another’s"
1727 "houses at your own cost turn and turn about. If, on the other hand, you"
1727 "elect to persist in spunging upon one man, heaven help me, but Jove"
1727 "shall reckon with you in full, and when you fall in my father’s house"
1727 "there shall be no man to avenge you.”"
1727 ""
1727 "As he spoke Jove sent two eagles from the top of the mountain, and they"
1727 "flew on and on with the wind, sailing side by side in their own lordly"
1727 "flight. When they were right over the middle of the assembly they"
1727 "wheeled and circled about, beating the air with their wings and glaring"
1727 "death into the eyes of them that were below; then, fighting fiercely"
1727 "and tearing at one another, they flew off towards the right over the"
1727 "town. The people wondered as they saw them, and asked each other what"
1727 "all this might be; whereon Halitherses, who was the best prophet and"
1727 "reader of omens among them, spoke to them plainly and in all honesty,"
1727 "saying:"
1727 ""
1727 "“Hear me, men of Ithaca, and I speak more particularly to the suitors,"
1727 "for I see mischief brewing for them. Ulysses is not going to be away"
1727 "much longer; indeed he is close at hand to deal out death and"
1727 "destruction, not on them alone, but on many another of us who live in"
1727 "Ithaca. Let us then be wise in time, and put a stop to this wickedness"
1727 "before he comes. Let the suitors do so of their own accord; it will be"
1727 "better for them, for I am not prophesying without due knowledge;"
1727 "everything has happened to Ulysses as I foretold when the Argives set"
1727 "out for Troy, and he with them. I said that after going through much"
1727 "hardship and losing all his men he should come home again in the"
1727 "twentieth year and that no one would know him; and now all this is"
1727 "coming true.”"
1727 ""
1727 "Eurymachus son of Polybus then said, “Go home, old man, and prophesy to"
1727 "your own children, or it may be worse for them. I can read these omens"
1727 "myself much better than you can; birds are always flying about in the"

1727 "it, for the suitors will prevent this—and in the next, we will lay a"

1727 "TELEMACHUS VISITS NESTOR AT PYLOS."
1727 ""
1727 ""
1727 "but as the sun was rising from the fair sea24 into the firmament of"
1727 "heaven to shed light on mortals and immortals, they reached Pylos the"
1727 "city of Neleus. Now the people of Pylos were gathered on the sea shore"
1727 "to offer sacrifice of black bulls to Neptune lord of the Earthquake."
1727 "There were nine guilds with five hundred men in each, and there were"
1727 "nine bulls to each guild. As they were eating the inward meats25 and"
1727 "burning the thigh bones [on the embers] in the name of Neptune,"
1727 "Telemachus and his crew arrived, furled their sails, brought their ship"
1727 "to anchor, and went ashore."
1727 ""
1727 "Minerva led the way and Telemachus followed her. Presently she said,"
1727 "“Telemachus, you must not be in the least shy or nervous; you have"
1727 "taken this voyage to try and find out where your father is buried and"
1727 "how he came by his end; so go straight up to Nestor that we may see"
1727 "what he has got to tell us. Beg of him to speak the truth, and he will"
1727 "tell no lies, for he is an excellent person.”"
1727 ""
1727 "“But how, Mentor,” replied Telemachus, “dare I go up to Nestor, and how"
1727 "am I to address him? I have never yet been used to holding long"
1727 "conversations with people, and am ashamed to begin questioning one who"
1727 "is so much older than myself.”"
1727 ""
1727 "“Some things, Telemachus,” answered Minerva, “will be suggested to you"
1727 "by your own instinct, and heaven will prompt you further; for I am"
1727 "assured that the gods have been with you from the time of your birth"
1727 "until now.”"
1727 ""
1727 "She then went quickly on, and Telemachus followed in her steps till"
1727 "they reached the place where the guilds of the Pylian people were"
1727 "assembled. There they found Nestor sitting with his sons, while his"
1727 "company round him were busy getting dinner ready, and putting pieces of"
1727 "meat on to the spits26 while other pieces were cooking. When they saw"
1727 "the strangers they crowded round them, took them by the hand and bade"
1727 "them take their places. Nestor’s son Pisistratus at once offered his"
1727 "hand to each of them, and seated them on some soft sheepskins that were"
1727 "lying on the sands near his father and his brother Thrasymedes. Then he"
1727 "gave them their portions of the inward meats and poured wine for them"
1727 "into a golden cup, handing it to Minerva first, and saluting her at the"
1727 "same time."
1727 ""
1727 "“Offer a prayer, sir,” said he, “to King Neptune, for it is his feast"
1727 "that you are joining; when you have duly prayed and made your drink"
1727 "offering, pass the cup to your friend that he may do so also. I doubt"
1727 "not that he too lifts his hands in prayer, for man cannot live without"
1727 "God in the world. Still he is younger than you are, and is much of an"
1727 "age with myself, so I will give you the precedence.”"
1727 ""
1727 "As he spoke he handed her the cup. Minerva thought it very right and"
1727 "proper of him to have given it to herself first;27 she accordingly"
1727 "began praying heartily to Neptune. “O thou,” she cried, “that"
1727 "encirclest the earth, vouchsafe to grant the prayers of thy servants"
1727 "that call upon thee. More especially we pray thee send down thy grace"
1727 "on Nestor and on his sons; thereafter also make the rest of the Pylian"
1727 "people some handsome return for the goodly hecatomb they are offering"
1727 "you. Lastly, grant Telemachus and myself a happy issue, in respect of"
1727 "the matter that has brought us in our ship to Pylos.”"
1727 ""
1727 "When she had thus made an end of praying, she handed the cup to"
1727 "Telemachus and he prayed likewise. By and by, when the outer meats were"
1727 "roasted and had been taken off the spits, the carvers gave every man"
1727 "his portion and they all made an excellent dinner. As soon as they had"
1727 "had enough to eat and drink, Nestor, knight of Gerene, began to speak."
1727 ""
1727 "“Now,” said he, “that our guests have done their dinner, it will be"
1727 "best to ask them who they are. Who, then, sir strangers, are you, and"
1727 "from what port have you sailed? Are you traders? or do you sail the"
1727 "seas as rovers with your hand against every man, and every man’s hand"
1727 "against you?”"
1727 ""
1727 "Telemachus answered boldly, for Minerva had given him courage to ask"
1727 "about his father and get himself a good name."
1727 ""
1727 "“Nestor,” said he, “son of Neleus, honour to the Achaean name, you ask"
1727 "whence we come, and I will tell you. We come from Ithaca under"
1727 "Neritum,28 and the matter about which I would speak is of private not"
1727 "public import. I seek news of my unhappy father Ulysses, who is said to"
1727 "have sacked the town of Troy in company with yourself. We know what"
1727 "fate befell each one of the other heroes who fought at Troy, but as"
1727 "regards Ulysses heaven has hidden from us the knowledge even that he is"
1727 "dead at all, for no one can certify us in what place he perished, nor"
1727 "say whether he fell in battle on the mainland, or was lost at sea amid"
1727 "the waves of Amphitrite. Therefore I am suppliant at your knees, if"
1727 "haply you may be pleased to tell me of his melancholy end, whether you"
1727 "saw it with your own eyes, or heard it from some other traveller, for"
1727 "he was a man born to trouble. Do not soften things out of any pity for"
1727 "me, but tell me in all plainness exactly what you saw. If my brave"
1727 "father Ulysses ever did you loyal service, either by word or deed, when"
1727 "you Achaeans were harassed among the Trojans, bear it in mind now as in"
1727 "my favour and tell me truly all.”"
1727 ""
1727 "“My friend,” answered Nestor, “you recall a time of much sorrow to my"
1727 "mind, for the brave Achaeans suffered much both at sea, while"
1727 "privateering under Achilles, and when fighting before the great city of"
1727 "king Priam. Our best men all of them fell there—Ajax, Achilles,"
1727 "Patroclus peer of gods in counsel, and my own dear son Antilochus, a"
1727 "man singularly fleet of foot and in fight valiant. But we suffered much"
1727 "more than this; what mortal tongue indeed could tell the whole story?"
1727 "Though you were to stay here and question me for five years, or even"
1727 "six, I could not tell you all that the Achaeans suffered, and you would"
1727 "turn homeward weary of my tale before it ended. Nine long years did we"
1727 "try every kind of stratagem, but the hand of heaven was against us;"
1727 "during all this time there was no one who could compare with your"
1727 "father in subtlety—if indeed you are his son—I can hardly believe my"
1727 "eyes—and you talk just like him too—no one would say that people of"
1727 "such different ages could speak so much alike. He and I never had any"
1727 "kind of difference from first to last neither in camp nor council, but"
1727 "in singleness of heart and purpose we advised the Argives how all might"
1727 "be ordered for the best."
1727 ""
1727 "“When, however, we had sacked the city of Priam, and were setting sail"

1727 "Argives on their homeward voyage; for they had not all been either wise"
1727 "or understanding, and hence many came to a bad end through the"
1727 "displeasure of Jove’s daughter Minerva, who brought about a quarrel"
1727 "between the two sons of Atreus."
1727 ""
1727 "“The sons of Atreus called a meeting which was not as it should be, for"
1727 "it was sunset and the Achaeans were heavy with wine. When they"
1727 "explained why they had called the people together, it seemed that"
1727 "Menelaus was for sailing homeward at once, and this displeased"
1727 "Agamemnon, who thought that we should wait till we had offered"
1727 "hecatombs to appease the anger of Minerva. Fool that he was, he might"
1727 "have known that he would not prevail with her, for when the gods have"
1727 "made up their minds they do not change them lightly. So the two stood"
1727 "bandying hard words, whereon the Achaeans sprang to their feet with a"
1727 "cry that rent the air, and were of two minds as to what they should do."
1727 ""
1727 "“That night we rested and nursed our anger, for Jove was hatching"
1727 "mischief against us. But in the morning some of us drew our ships into"
1727 "the water and put our goods with our women on board, while the rest,"
1727 "about half in number, stayed behind with Agamemnon. We—the other"
1727 "half—embarked and sailed; and the ships went well, for heaven had"
1727 "smoothed the sea. When we reached Tenedos we offered sacrifices to the"
1727 "gods, for we were longing to get home; cruel Jove, however, did not yet"
1727 "mean that we should do so, and raised a second quarrel in the course of"
1727 "which some among us turned their ships back again, and sailed away"
1727 "under Ulysses to make their peace with Agamemnon; but I, and all the"
1727 "ships that were with me pressed forward, for I saw that mischief was"
1727 "brewing. The son of Tydeus went on also with me, and his crews with"
1727 "him. Later on Menelaus joined us at Lesbos, and found us making up our"
1727 "minds about our course—for we did not know whether to go outside Chios"
1727 "by the island of Psyra, keeping this to our left, or inside Chios, over"
1727 "against the stormy headland of Mimas. So we asked heaven for a sign,"
1727 "and were shown one to the effect that we should be soonest out of"
1727 "danger if we headed our ships across the open sea to Euboea. This we"
1727 "therefore did, and a fair wind sprang up which gave us a quick passage"
1727 "during the night to Geraestus,29 where we offered many sacrifices to"
1727 "Neptune for having helped us so far on our way. Four days later Diomed"
1727 "and his men stationed their ships in Argos, but I held on for Pylos,"
1727 "and the wind never fell light from the day when heaven first made it"
1727 "fair for me."
1727 ""
1727 "“Therefore, my dear young friend, I returned without hearing anything"
1727 "about the others. I know neither who got home safely nor who were lost"
1727 "but, as in duty bound, I will give you without reserve the reports that"
1727 "have reached me since I have been here in my own house. They say the"

1727 "did the valiant son of Poias, Philoctetes. Idomeneus, again, lost no"
1727 "men at sea, and all his followers who escaped death in the field got"
1727 "safe home with him to Crete. No matter how far out of the world you"
1727 "live, you will have heard of Agamemnon and the bad end he came to at"
1727 "the hands of Aegisthus—and a fearful reckoning did Aegisthus presently"
1727 "pay. See what a good thing it is for a man to leave a son behind him to"
1727 "do as Orestes did, who killed false Aegisthus the murderer of his noble"
1727 "father. You too, then—for you are a tall smart-looking fellow—show your"
1727 "mettle and make yourself a name in story.”"
1727 ""
1727 "“Nestor son of Neleus,” answered Telemachus, “honour to the Achaean"
1727 "name, the Achaeans applaud Orestes and his name will live through all"
1727 "time for he has avenged his father nobly. Would that heaven might grant"
1727 "me to do like vengeance on the insolence of the wicked suitors, who are"
1727 "ill treating me and plotting my ruin; but the gods have no such"
1727 "happiness in store for me and for my father, so we must bear it as best"

1727 ""
1727 "“My friend,” said Nestor, “now that you remind me, I remember to have"
1727 "heard that your mother has many suitors, who are ill disposed towards"
1727 "you and are making havoc of your estate. Do you submit to this tamely,"

1727 "CALYPSO—ULYSSES REACHES SCHERIA ON A RAFT."

1727 ""
1727 "And now, as Dawn rose from her couch beside Tithonus—harbinger of light"
1727 "alike to mortals and immortals—the gods met in council and with them,"
1727 "Jove the lord of thunder, who is their king. Thereon Minerva began to"
1727 "tell them of the many sufferings of Ulysses, for she pitied him away"
1727 "there in the house of the nymph Calypso."
1727 ""
1727 "“Father Jove,” said she, “and all you other gods that live in"
1727 "everlasting bliss, I hope there may never be such a thing as a kind and"
1727 "well-disposed ruler any more, nor one who will govern equitably. I hope"
1727 "they will be all henceforth cruel and unjust, for there is not one of"
1727 "his subjects but has forgotten Ulysses, who ruled them as though he"
1727 "were their father. There he is, lying in great pain in an island where"
1727 "dwells the nymph Calypso, who will not let him go; and he cannot get"
1727 "back to his own country, for he can find neither ships nor sailors to"
1727 "take him over the sea. Furthermore, wicked people are now trying to"
1727 "murder his only son Telemachus, who is coming home from Pylos and"
1727 "Lacedaemon, where he has been to see if he can get news of his father.”"
1727 ""
1727 "“What, my dear, are you talking about?” replied her father, “did you"
1727 "not send him there yourself, because you thought it would help Ulysses"
1727 "to get home and punish the suitors? Besides, you are perfectly able to"
1727 "protect Telemachus, and to see him safely home again, while the suitors"
1727 "have to come hurry-skurrying back without having killed him.”"
1727 ""
1727 "When he had thus spoken, he said to his son Mercury, “Mercury, you are"

1727 "men, but after a perilous voyage of twenty days upon a raft he is to"

1727 "kin to the gods, and will honour him as though he were one of"
1727 "ourselves. They will send him in a ship to his own country, and will"

1727 ""
1727 "Thus he spoke, and Mercury, guide and guardian, slayer of Argus, did as"
1727 "he was told. Forthwith he bound on his glittering golden sandals with"

1727 ""
1727 "He found her at home. There was a large fire burning on the hearth, and"
1727 "one could smell from far the fragrant reek of burning cedar and sandal"

1727 "Even a god could not help being charmed with such a lovely spot, so"

1727 ""

1727 ""

1727 ""
1727 "In four days he had completed the whole work, and on the fifth Calypso"

1727 "will become of me? I am afraid Calypso was right when she said I should"

1727 "black is Jove making heaven with his clouds, and what a sea the winds"
1727 "are raising from every quarter at once. I am now safe to perish. Blest"
1727 "and thrice blest were those Danaans who fell before Troy in the cause"

1727 "Trojans were pressing me so sorely about the dead body of Achilles, for"
1727 "then I should have had due burial and the Achaeans would have honoured"
1727 "my name; but now it seems that I shall come to a most pitiable end.”"
1727 ""
1727 "As he spoke a sea broke over him with such terrific fury that the raft"
1727 "reeled again, and he was carried overboard a long way off. He let go"
1727 "the helm, and the force of the hurricane was so great that it broke the"
1727 "mast half way up, and both sail and yard went over into the sea. For a"

1727 "down; but at last he got his head above water and spat out the bitter"

1727 "escape drowning. The sea took the raft and tossed it about as Autumn"
1727 "winds whirl thistledown round and round upon a road. It was as though"
1727 "the South, North, East, and West winds were all playing battledore and"
1727 "shuttlecock with it at once."

1727 ""

1727 ""

1727 "THE MEETING BETWEEN NAUSICAA AND ULYSSES."
1727 ""
1727 ""
1727 "So here Ulysses slept, overcome by sleep and toil; but Minerva went off"
1727 "to the country and city of the Phaeacians—a people who used to live in"
1727 "the fair town of Hypereia, near the lawless Cyclopes. Now the Cyclopes"
1727 "were stronger than they and plundered them, so their king Nausithous"
1727 "moved them thence and settled them in Scheria, far from all other"
1727 "people. He surrounded the city with a wall, built houses and temples,"
1727 "and divided the lands among his people; but he was dead and gone to the"
1727 "house of Hades, and King Alcinous, whose counsels were inspired of"
1727 "heaven, was now reigning. To his house, then, did Minerva hie in"
1727 "furtherance of the return of Ulysses."
1727 ""
1727 "She went straight to the beautifully decorated bedroom in which there"
1727 "slept a girl who was as lovely as a goddess, Nausicaa, daughter to King"
1727 "Alcinous. Two maid servants were sleeping near her, both very pretty,"
1727 "one on either side of the doorway, which was closed with well made"
1727 "folding doors. Minerva took the form of the famous sea captain Dymas’s"
1727 "daughter, who was a bosom friend of Nausicaa and just her own age;"
1727 "then, coming up to the girl’s bedside like a breath of wind, she"
1727 "hovered over her head and said:"
1727 ""
1727 "“Nausicaa, what can your mother have been about, to have such a lazy"
1727 "daughter? Here are your clothes all lying in disorder, yet you are"
1727 "going to be married almost immediately, and should not only be well"
1727 "dressed yourself, but should find good clothes for those who attend"
1727 "you. This is the way to get yourself a good name, and to make your"
1727 "father and mother proud of you. Suppose, then, that we make tomorrow a"
1727 "washing day, and start at daybreak. I will come and help you so that"
1727 "you may have everything ready as soon as possible, for all the best"
1727 "young men among your own people are courting you, and you are not going"
1727 "to remain a maid much longer. Ask your father, therefore, to have a"
1727 "waggon and mules ready for us at daybreak, to take the rugs, robes, and"
1727 "girdles, and you can ride, too, which will be much pleasanter for you"
1727 "than walking, for the washing-cisterns are some way from the town.”"
1727 ""
1727 "When she had said this Minerva went away to Olympus, which they say is"
1727 "the everlasting home of the gods. Here no wind beats roughly, and"
1727 "neither rain nor snow can fall; but it abides in everlasting sunshine"
1727 "and in a great peacefulness of light, wherein the blessed gods are"
1727 "illumined for ever and ever. This was the place to which the goddess"
1727 "went when she had given instructions to the girl."
1727 ""
1727 "By and by morning came and woke Nausicaa, who began wondering about her"
1727 "dream; she therefore went to the other end of the house to tell her"
1727 "father and mother all about it, and found them in their own room. Her"

1727 ""

1727 "to take all our dirty clothes to the river and wash them. You are the"

1727 "at home, two of them married, while the other three are good looking"

1727 ""
1727 "She did not say a word about her own wedding, for she did not like to,"
1727 "but her father knew and said, “You shall have the mules, my love, and"
1727 "whatever else you have a mind for. Be off with you, and the men shall"

1727 ""
1727 "On this he gave his orders to the servants, who got the waggon out,"
1727 "harnessed the mules, and put them to, while the girl brought the"

1727 ""
1727 "When they reached the water side they went to the washing cisterns,"

1727 "When it was time for them to start home, and they were folding the"

1727 "will see the market place with a temple of Neptune in the middle of it,"
1727 "and paved with large stones bedded in the earth. Here people deal in"
1727 "ship’s gear of all kinds, such as cables and sails, and here, too, are"
1727 "the places where oars are made, for the Phaeacians are not a nation of"
1727 "archers; they know nothing about bows and arrows, but are a sea-faring"
1727 "folk, and pride themselves on their masts, oars, and ships, with which"
1727 "they travel far over the sea."
1727 ""
1727 "“I am afraid of the gossip and scandal that may be set on foot against"
1727 "me later on; for the people here are very ill-natured, and some low"
1727 "fellow, if he met us, might say, ‘Who is this fine-looking stranger"
1727 "that is going about with Nausicaa? Where did she find him? I suppose"
1727 "she is going to marry him. Perhaps he is a vagabond sailor whom she has"
1727 "taken from some foreign vessel, for we have no neighbours; or some god"
1727 "has at last come down from heaven in answer to her prayers, and she is"
1727 "going to live with him all the rest of her life. It would be a good"
1727 "thing if she would take herself off and find a husband somewhere else,"
1727 "for she will not look at one of the many excellent young Phaeacians who"
1727 "are in love with her.’ This is the kind of disparaging remark that"
1727 "would be made about me, and I could not complain, for I should myself"
1727 "be scandalised at seeing any other girl do the like, and go about with"
1727 "men in spite of everybody, while her father and mother were still"
1727 "alive, and without having been married in the face of all the world."
1727 ""
1727 "“If, therefore, you want my father to give you an escort and to help"
1727 "you home, do as I bid you; you will see a beautiful grove of poplars by"
1727 "the road side dedicated to Minerva; it has a well in it and a meadow"
1727 "all round it. Here my father has a field of rich garden ground, about"
1727 "as far from the town as a man’s voice will carry. Sit down there and"
1727 "wait for a while till the rest of us can get into the town and reach my"
1727 "father’s house. Then, when you think we must have done this, come into"
1727 "the town and ask the way to the house of my father Alcinous. You will"
1727 "have no difficulty in finding it; any child will point it out to you,"
1727 "for no one else in the whole town has anything like such a fine house"
1727 "as he has. When you have got past the gates and through the outer"
1727 "court, go right across the inner court till you come to my mother. You"
1727 "will find her sitting by the fire and spinning her purple wool by"
1727 "firelight. It is a fine sight to see her as she leans back against one"
1727 "of the bearing-posts with her maids all ranged behind her. Close to her"
1727 "seat stands that of my father, on which he sits and topes like an"
1727 "immortal god. Never mind him, but go up to my mother, and lay your"
1727 "hands upon her knees if you would get home quickly. If you can gain her"
1727 "over, you may hope to see your own country again, no matter how distant"
1727 "it may be.”"
1727 ""
1727 "So saying she lashed the mules with her whip and they left the river."
1727 "The mules drew well, and their hoofs went up and down upon the road."
1727 "She was careful not to go too fast for Ulysses and the maids who were"
1727 "following on foot along with the waggon, so she plied her whip with"
1727 "judgement. As the sun was going down they came to the sacred grove of"
1727 "Minerva, and there Ulysses sat down and prayed to the mighty daughter"
1727 "of Jove."
1727 ""
1727 "“Hear me,” he cried, “daughter of Aegis-bearing Jove, unweariable, hear"
1727 "me now, for you gave no heed to my prayers when Neptune was wrecking"
1727 "me. Now, therefore, have pity upon me and grant that I may find friends"
1727 "and be hospitably received by the Phaeacians.”"
1727 ""
1727 "Thus did he pray, and Minerva heard his prayer, but she would not show"
1727 "herself to him openly, for she was afraid of her uncle Neptune, who was"
1727 "still furious in his endeavors to prevent Ulysses from getting home."

1727 "RECEPTION OF ULYSSES AT THE PALACE OF KING ALCINOUS."
1727 ""
1727 ""
1727 "Thus, then, did Ulysses wait and pray; but the girl drove on to the"
1727 "town. When she reached her father’s house she drew up at the gateway,"
1727 "and her brothers—comely as the gods—gathered round her, took the mules"
1727 "out of the waggon, and carried the clothes into the house, while she"
1727 "went to her own room, where an old servant, Eurymedusa of Apeira, lit"
1727 "the fire for her. This old woman had been brought by sea from Apeira,"
1727 "and had been chosen as a prize for Alcinous because he was king over"
1727 "the Phaeacians, and the people obeyed him as though he were a god.57"
1727 "She had been nurse to Nausicaa, and had now lit the fire for her, and"
1727 "brought her supper for her into her own room."
1727 ""
1727 "Presently Ulysses got up to go towards the town; and Minerva shed a"
1727 "thick mist all round him to hide him in case any of the proud"
1727 "Phaeacians who met him should be rude to him, or ask him who he was."
1727 "Then, as he was just entering the town, she came towards him in the"
1727 "likeness of a little girl carrying a pitcher. She stood right in front"
1727 "of him, and Ulysses said:"
1727 ""
1727 "“My dear, will you be so kind as to show me the house of king Alcinous?"
1727 "I am an unfortunate foreigner in distress, and do not know one in your"
1727 "town and country.”"
1727 ""
1727 "Then Minerva said, “Yes, father stranger, I will show you the house you"
1727 "want, for Alcinous lives quite close to my own father. I will go before"
1727 "you and show the way, but say not a word as you go, and do not look at"
1727 "any man, nor ask him questions; for the people here cannot abide"
1727 "strangers, and do not like men who come from some other place. They are"
1727 "a sea-faring folk, and sail the seas by the grace of Neptune in ships"
1727 "that glide along like thought, or as a bird in the air.”"
1727 ""
1727 "On this she led the way, and Ulysses followed in her steps; but not one"
1727 "of the Phaeacians could see him as he passed through the city in the"
1727 "midst of them; for the great goddess Minerva in her good will towards"
1727 "him had hidden him in a thick cloud of darkness. He admired their"
1727 "harbours, ships, places of assembly, and the lofty walls of the city,"
1727 "which, with the palisade on top of them, were very striking, and when"
1727 "they reached the king’s house Minerva said:"
1727 ""
1727 "“This is the house, father stranger, which you would have me show you."
1727 "You will find a number of great people sitting at table, but do not be"
1727 "afraid; go straight in, for the bolder a man is the more likely he is"
1727 "to carry his point, even though he is a stranger. First find the queen."
1727 "Her name is Arete, and she comes of the same family as her husband"
1727 "Alcinous. They both descend originally from Neptune, who was father to"
1727 "Nausithous by Periboea, a woman of great beauty. Periboea was the"
1727 "youngest daughter of Eurymedon, who at one time reigned over the"
1727 "giants, but he ruined his ill-fated people and lost his own life to"
1727 "boot."
1727 ""
1727 "“Neptune, however, lay with his daughter, and she had a son by him, the"
1727 "great Nausithous, who reigned over the Phaeacians. Nausithous had two"
1727 "sons Rhexenor and Alcinous;58 Apollo killed the first of them while he"
1727 "was still a bridegroom and without male issue; but he left a daughter"
1727 "Arete, whom Alcinous married, and honours as no other woman is honoured"
1727 "of all those that keep house along with their husbands."
1727 ""
1727 "“Thus she both was, and still is, respected beyond measure by her"
1727 "children, by Alcinous himself, and by the whole people, who look upon"
1727 "her as a goddess, and greet her whenever she goes about the city, for"
1727 "she is a thoroughly good woman both in head and heart, and when any"
1727 "women are friends of hers, she will help their husbands also to settle"
1727 "their disputes. If you can gain her good will, you may have every hope"
1727 "of seeing your friends again, and getting safely back to your home and"
1727 "country.”"
1727 ""
1727 "Then Minerva left Scheria and went away over the sea. She went to"
1727 "Marathon59 and to the spacious streets of Athens, where she entered the"
1727 "abode of Erechtheus; but Ulysses went on to the house of Alcinous, and"
1727 "he pondered much as he paused a while before reaching the threshold of"
1727 "bronze, for the splendour of the palace was like that of the sun or"
1727 "moon. The walls on either side were of bronze from end to end, and the"
1727 "cornice was of blue enamel. The doors were gold, and hung on pillars of"
1727 "silver that rose from a floor of bronze, while the lintel was silver"
1727 "and the hook of the door was of gold."
1727 ""
1727 "On either side there stood gold and silver mastiffs which Vulcan, with"
1727 "his consummate skill, had fashioned expressly to keep watch over the"
1727 "palace of king Alcinous; so they were immortal and could never grow"
1727 "old. Seats were ranged all along the wall, here and there from one end"
1727 "to the other, with coverings of fine woven work which the women of the"
1727 "house had made. Here the chief persons of the Phaeacians used to sit"
1727 "and eat and drink, for there was abundance at all seasons; and there"
1727 "were golden figures of young men with lighted torches in their hands,"
1727 "raised on pedestals, to give light by night to those who were at table."
1727 "There are60 fifty maid servants in the house, some of whom are always"
1727 "grinding rich yellow grain at the mill, while others work at the loom,"
1727 "or sit and spin, and their shuttles go backwards and forwards like the"
1727 "fluttering of aspen leaves, while the linen is so closely woven that it"
1727 "will turn oil. As the Phaeacians are the best sailors in the world, so"
1727 "their women excel all others in weaving, for Minerva has taught them"
1727 "all manner of useful arts, and they are very intelligent."
1727 ""
1727 "Outside the gate of the outer court there is a large garden of about"
1727 "four acres with a wall all round it. It is full of beautiful"
1727 "trees—pears, pomegranates, and the most delicious apples. There are"
1727 "luscious figs also, and olives in full growth. The fruits never rot nor"
1727 "fail all the year round, neither winter nor summer, for the air is so"
1727 "soft that a new crop ripens before the old has dropped. Pear grows on"
1727 "pear, apple on apple, and fig on fig, and so also with the grapes, for"
1727 "there is an excellent vineyard: on the level ground of a part of this,"
1727 "the grapes are being made into raisins; in another part they are being"
1727 "gathered; some are being trodden in the wine tubs, others further on"
1727 "have shed their blossom and are beginning to show fruit, others again"
1727 "are just changing colour. In the furthest part of the ground there are"
1727 "beautifully arranged beds of flowers that are in bloom all the year"
1727 "round. Two streams go through it, the one turned in ducts throughout"
1727 "the whole garden, while the other is carried under the ground of the"
1727 "outer court to the house itself, and the town’s people draw water from"
1727 "it. Such, then, were the splendours with which the gods had endowed the"
1727 "house of king Alcinous."
1727 ""
1727 "So here Ulysses stood for a while and looked about him, but when he had"
1727 "looked long enough he crossed the threshold and went within the"
1727 "precincts of the house. There he found all the chief people among the"
1727 "Phaeacians making their drink offerings to Mercury, which they always"
1727 "did the last thing before going away for the night.61 He went straight"
1727 "through the court, still hidden by the cloak of darkness in which"
1727 "Minerva had enveloped him, till he reached Arete and King Alcinous;"
1727 "then he laid his hands upon the knees of the queen, and at that moment"
1727 "the miraculous darkness fell away from him and he became visible. Every"
1727 "one was speechless with surprise at seeing a man there, but Ulysses"
1727 "began at once with his petition."
1727 ""
1727 "“Queen Arete,” he exclaimed, “daughter of great Rhexenor, in my"
1727 "distress I humbly pray you, as also your husband and these your guests"
1727 "(whom may heaven prosper with long life and happiness, and may they"
1727 "leave their possessions to their children, and all the honours"
1727 "conferred upon them by the state) to help me home to my own country as"
1727 "soon as possible; for I have been long in trouble and away from my"
1727 "friends.”"
1727 ""
1727 "Then he sat down on the hearth among the ashes and they all held their"
1727 "peace, till presently the old hero Echeneus, who was an excellent"
1727 "speaker and an elder among the Phaeacians, plainly and in all honesty"
1727 "addressed them thus:"
1727 ""
1727 "“Alcinous,” said he, “it is not creditable to you that a stranger"
1727 "should be seen sitting among the ashes of your hearth; every one is"
1727 "waiting to hear what you are about to say; tell him, then, to rise and"
1727 "take a seat on a stool inlaid with silver, and bid your servants mix"
1727 "some wine and water that we may make a drink offering to Jove the lord"
1727 "of thunder, who takes all well disposed suppliants under his"
1727 "protection; and let the housekeeper give him some supper, of whatever"
1727 "there may be in the house.”"
1727 ""
1727 "When Alcinous heard this he took Ulysses by the hand, raised him from"
1727 "the hearth, and bade him take the seat of Laodamas, who had been"
1727 "sitting beside him, and was his favourite son. A maid servant then"

1727 "good things of what there was in the house, and Ulysses ate and drank."
1727 "Then Alcinous said to one of the servants, “Pontonous, mix a cup of"
1727 "wine and hand it round that we may make drink-offerings to Jove the"
1727 "lord of thunder, who is the protector of all well-disposed suppliants.”"
1727 ""
1727 "Pontonous then mixed wine and water, and handed it round after giving"
1727 "every man his drink-offering. When they had made their offerings, and"
1727 "had drunk each as much as he was minded, Alcinous said:"
1727 ""
1727 "“Aldermen and town councillors of the Phaeacians, hear my words. You"
1727 "have had your supper, so now go home to bed. To-morrow morning I shall"
1727 "invite a still larger number of aldermen, and will give a sacrificial"
1727 "banquet in honour of our guest; we can then discuss the question of his"
1727 "escort, and consider how we may at once send him back rejoicing to his"
1727 "own country without trouble or inconvenience to himself, no matter how"
1727 "distant it may be. We must see that he comes to no harm while on his"

1727 "possible, however, that the stranger is one of the immortals who has"

1727 "departing from their usual practice, for hitherto they have made"
1727 "themselves perfectly clear to us when we have been offering them"
1727 "hecatombs. They come and sit at our feasts just like one of our selves,"

1727 "gods as the Cyclopes and the savage giants are.”62"
1727 ""
1727 "Then Ulysses said: “Pray, Alcinous, do not take any such notion into"
1727 "your head. I have nothing of the immortal about me, neither in body nor"
1727 "mind, and most resemble those among you who are the most afflicted."
1727 "Indeed, were I to tell you all that heaven has seen fit to lay upon me,"

1727 "let me sup in spite of sorrow, for an empty stomach is a very"
1727 "importunate thing, and thrusts itself on a man’s notice no matter how"
1727 "dire is his distress. I am in great trouble, yet it insists that I"
1727 "shall eat and drink, bids me lay aside all memory of my sorrows and"
1727 "dwell only on the due replenishing of itself. As for yourselves, do as"
1727 "you propose, and at break of day set about helping me to get home. I"

1727 "bondsmen, and all the greatness of my house.”63"
1727 ""
1727 "Thus did he speak. Every one approved his saying, and agreed that he"
1727 "should have his escort inasmuch as he had spoken reasonably. Then when"
1727 "they had made their drink offerings, and had drunk each as much as he"
1727 "was minded they went home to bed every man in his own abode, leaving"

1727 "Did you not say you had come here from beyond the sea?”"
1727 ""
1727 "And Ulysses answered, “It would be a long story Madam, were I to relate"
1727 "in full the tale of my misfortunes, for the hand of heaven has been"
1727 "laid heavy upon me; but as regards your question, there is an island"
1727 "far away in the sea which is called ‘the Ogygian.’ Here dwells the"

1727 "brought me to her hearth all desolate and alone, for Jove struck my"
1727 "ship with his thunderbolts, and broke it up in mid-ocean. My brave"
1727 "comrades were drowned every man of them, but I stuck to the keel and"
1727 "was carried hither and thither for the space of nine days, till at last"
1727 "during the darkness of the tenth night the gods brought me to the"
1727 "Ogygian island where the great goddess Calypso lives. She took me in"
1727 "and treated me with the utmost kindness; indeed she wanted to make me"
1727 "immortal that I might never grow old, but she could not persuade me to"
1727 "let her do so."
1727 ""
1727 "“I stayed with Calypso seven years straight on end, and watered the"
1727 "good clothes she gave me with my tears during the whole time; but at"

1727 "high that I could no longer keep to my raft, which went to pieces under"
1727 "the fury of the gale, and I had to swim for it, till wind and current"
1727 "brought me to your shores."
1727 ""
1727 "“There I tried to land, but could not, for it was a bad place and the"
1727 "waves dashed me against the rocks, so I again took to the sea and swam"
1727 "on till I came to a river that seemed the most likely landing place,"
1727 "for there were no rocks and it was sheltered from the wind. Here, then,"
1727 "I got out of the water and gathered my senses together again. Night was"
1727 "coming on, so I left the river, and went into a thicket, where I"
1727 "covered myself all over with leaves, and presently heaven sent me off"

1727 "leaves all night, and through the next day till afternoon, when I woke"
1727 "as the sun was westering, and saw your daughter’s maid servants playing"
1727 "upon the beach, and your daughter among them looking like a goddess. I"
1727 "besought her aid, and she proved to be of an excellent disposition,"
1727 "much more so than could be expected from so young a person—for young"
1727 "people are apt to be thoughtless. She gave me plenty of bread and wine,"
1727 "and when she had had me washed in the river she also gave me the"
1727 "clothes in which you see me. Now, therefore, though it has pained me to"
1727 "do so, I have told you the whole truth.”"
1727 ""
1727 "Then Alcinous said, “Stranger, it was very wrong of my daughter not to"
1727 "bring you on at once to my house along with the maids, seeing that she"
1727 "was the first person whose aid you asked.”"
1727 ""
1727 "“Pray do not scold her,” replied Ulysses; “she is not to blame. She did"
1727 "tell me to follow along with the maids, but I was ashamed and afraid,"
1727 "for I thought you might perhaps be displeased if you saw me. Every"
1727 "human being is sometimes a little suspicious and irritable.”"
1727 ""
1727 "“Stranger,” replied Alcinous, “I am not the kind of man to get angry"
1727 "about nothing; it is always better to be reasonable; but by Father"
1727 "Jove, Minerva, and Apollo, now that I see what kind of person you are,"
1727 "and how much you think as I do, I wish you would stay here, marry my"
1727 "daughter, and become my son-in-law. If you will stay I will give you a"

1727 "tomorrow to the matter of your escort. You can sleep64 during the whole"
1727 "voyage if you like, and the men shall sail you over smooth waters"

1727 "voyage in a single day without distressing themselves, and came back"
1727 "again afterwards. You will thus see how much my ships excel all others,"

1727 ""
1727 "Then was Ulysses glad and prayed aloud saying, “Father Jove, grant that"
1727 "Alcinous may do all as he has said, for so he will win an imperishable"
1727 "name among mankind, and at the same time I shall return to my country.”"
1727 ""
1727 "Thus did they converse. Then Arete told her maids to set a bed in the"
1727 "room that was in the gatehouse, and make it with good red rugs, and to"
1727 "spread coverlets on the top of them with woollen cloaks for Ulysses to"

1727 "they had made the bed they came up to Ulysses and said, “Rise, sir"
1727 "stranger, and come with us for your bed is ready,” and glad indeed was"
1727 "he to go to his rest."
1727 ""
1727 "So Ulysses slept in a bed placed in a room over the echoing gateway;"
1727 "but Alcinous lay in the inner part of the house, with the queen his"
1727 "wife by his side."

1727 "BANQUET IN THE HOUSE OF ALCINOUS—THE GAMES."
1727 ""
1727 ""
1727 "Now when the child of morning, rosy-fingered Dawn, appeared, Alcinous"
1727 "and Ulysses both rose, and Alcinous led the way to the Phaeacian place"
1727 "of assembly, which was near the ships. When they got there they sat"
1727 "down side by side on a seat of polished stone, while Minerva took the"
1727 "form of one of Alcinous’ servants, and went round the town in order to"
1727 "help Ulysses to get home. She went up to the citizens, man by man, and"
1727 "said, “Aldermen and town councillors of the Phaeacians, come to the"
1727 "assembly all of you and listen to the stranger who has just come off a"
1727 "long voyage to the house of King Alcinous; he looks like an immortal"
1727 "god.”"
1727 ""
1727 "With these words she made them all want to come, and they flocked to"
1727 "the assembly till seats and standing room were alike crowded. Every one"
1727 "was struck with the appearance of Ulysses, for Minerva had beautified"
1727 "him about the head and shoulders, making him look taller and stouter"
1727 "than he really was, that he might impress the Phaeacians favourably as"
1727 "being a very remarkable man, and might come off well in the many trials"
1727 "of skill to which they would challenge him. Then, when they were got"
1727 "together, Alcinous spoke:"
1727 ""
1727 "“Hear me,” said he, “aldermen and town councillors of the Phaeacians,"
1727 "that I may speak even as I am minded. This stranger, whoever he may be,"
1727 "has found his way to my house from somewhere or other either East or"
1727 "West. He wants an escort and wishes to have the matter settled. Let us"
1727 "then get one ready for him, as we have done for others before him;"
1727 "indeed, no one who ever yet came to my house has been able to complain"
1727 "of me for not speeding on his way soon enough. Let us draw a ship into"
1727 "the sea—one that has never yet made a voyage—and man her with two and"
1727 "fifty of our smartest young sailors. Then when you have made fast your"
1727 "oars each by his own seat, leave the ship and come to my house to"
1727 "prepare a feast.65 I will find you in everything. I am giving these"
1727 "instructions to the young men who will form the crew, for as regards"
1727 "you aldermen and town councillors, you will join me in entertaining our"
1727 "guest in the cloisters. I can take no excuses, and we will have"
1727 "Demodocus to sing to us; for there is no bard like him whatever he may"
1727 "choose to sing about.”"
1727 ""
1727 "Alcinous then led the way, and the others followed after, while a"
1727 "servant went to fetch Demodocus. The fifty-two picked oarsmen went to"
1727 "the sea shore as they had been told, and when they got there they drew"
