1727 "screams aloud and flings her arms about him as he lies gasping for"
1727 "breath and dying, but her enemies beat her from behind about the back"
1727 "and shoulders, and carry her off into slavery, to a life of labour and"
1727 "sorrow, and the beauty fades from her cheeks—even so piteously did"
1727 "Ulysses weep, but none of those present perceived his tears except"
1727 "Alcinous, who was sitting near him, and could hear the sobs and sighs"
1727 "that he was heaving. The king, therefore, at once rose and said:"
1727 ""
1727 "“Aldermen and town councillors of the Phaeacians, let Demodocus cease"
1727 "his song, for there are those present who do not seem to like it. From"
1727 "the moment that we had done supper and Demodocus began to sing, our"
1727 "guest has been all the time groaning and lamenting. He is evidently in"
1727 "great trouble, so let the bard leave off, that we may all enjoy"
1727 "ourselves, hosts and guest alike. This will be much more as it should"
1727 "be, for all these festivities, with the escort and the presents that we"
1727 "are making with so much good will are wholly in his honour, and any one"
1727 "with even a moderate amount of right feeling knows that he ought to"
1727 "treat a guest and a suppliant as though he were his own brother."
1727 ""
1727 "“Therefore, Sir, do you on your part affect no more concealment nor"
1727 "reserve in the matter about which I shall ask you; it will be more"
1727 "polite in you to give me a plain answer; tell me the name by which your"
1727 "father and mother over yonder used to call you, and by which you were"
1727 "known among your neighbours and fellow-citizens. There is no one,"
1727 "neither rich nor poor, who is absolutely without any name whatever, for"
1727 "people’s fathers and mothers give them names as soon as they are born."
1727 "Tell me also your country, nation, and city, that our ships may shape"
1727 "their purpose accordingly and take you there. For the Phaeacians have"
1727 "no pilots; their vessels have no rudders as those of other nations"
1727 "have, but the ships themselves understand what it is that we are"
1727 "thinking about and want; they know all the cities and countries in the"
1727 "whole world, and can traverse the sea just as well even when it is"
1727 "covered with mist and cloud, so that there is no danger of being"
1727 "wrecked or coming to any harm. Still I do remember hearing my father"
1727 "say that Neptune was angry with us for being too easy-going in the"
1727 "matter of giving people escorts. He said that one of these days he"
1727 "should wreck a ship of ours as it was returning from having escorted"
1727 "some one,74 and bury our city under a high mountain. This is what my"
1727 "father used to say, but whether the god will carry out his threat or no"
1727 "is a matter which he will decide for himself."
1727 ""
1727 "“And now, tell me and tell me true. Where have you been wandering, and"
1727 "in what countries have you travelled? Tell us of the peoples"
1727 "themselves, and of their cities—who were hostile, savage and"
1727 "uncivilised, and who, on the other hand, hospitable and humane. Tell us"
1727 "also why you are made so unhappy on hearing about the return of the"
1727 "Argive Danaans from Troy. The gods arranged all this, and sent them"
1727 "their misfortunes in order that future generations might have something"
1727 "to sing about. Did you lose some brave kinsman of your wife’s when you"
1727 "were before Troy? a son-in-law or father-in-law—which are the nearest"
1727 "relations a man has outside his own flesh and blood? or was it some"
1727 "brave and kindly-natured comrade—for a good friend is as dear to a man"
1727 "as his own brother?”"
1727 ""

1727 "ULYSSES DECLARES HIMSELF AND BEGINS HIS STORY—-THE CICONS, LOTOPHAGI,"
1727 "AND CYCLOPES."
1727 ""
1727 ""
1727 "And Ulysses answered, “King Alcinous, it is a good thing to hear a bard"
1727 "with such a divine voice as this man has. There is nothing better or"
1727 "more delightful than when a whole people make merry together, with the"
1727 "guests sitting orderly to listen, while the table is loaded with bread"
1727 "and meats, and the cup-bearer draws wine and fills his cup for every"
1727 "man. This is indeed as fair a sight as a man can see. Now, however,"
1727 "since you are inclined to ask the story of my sorrows, and rekindle my"
1727 "own sad memories in respect of them, I do not know how to begin, nor"
1727 "yet how to continue and conclude my tale, for the hand of heaven has"
1727 "been laid heavily upon me."
1727 ""
1727 "“Firstly, then, I will tell you my name that you too may know it, and"
1727 "one day, if I outlive this time of sorrow, may become my guests though"
1727 "I live so far away from all of you. I am Ulysses son of Laertes,"
1727 "renowned among mankind for all manner of subtlety, so that my fame"
1727 "ascends to heaven. I live in Ithaca, where there is a high mountain"
1727 "called Neritum, covered with forests; and not far from it there is a"
1727 "group of islands very near to one another—Dulichium, Same, and the"
1727 "wooded island of Zacynthus. It lies squat on the horizon, all highest"
1727 "up in the sea towards the sunset, while the others lie away from it"
1727 "towards dawn.75 It is a rugged island, but it breeds brave men, and my"
1727 "eyes know none that they better love to look upon. The goddess Calypso"
1727 "kept me with her in her cave, and wanted me to marry her, as did also"
1727 "the cunning Aeaean goddess Circe; but they could neither of them"
1727 "persuade me, for there is nothing dearer to a man than his own country"
1727 "and his parents, and however splendid a home he may have in a foreign"
1727 "country, if it be far from father or mother, he does not care about it."
1727 "Now, however, I will tell you of the many hazardous adventures which by"
1727 "Jove’s will I met with on my return from Troy."
1727 ""
1727 "“When I had set sail thence the wind took me first to Ismarus, which is"
1727 "the city of the Cicons. There I sacked the town and put the people to"
1727 "the sword. We took their wives and also much booty, which we divided"
1727 "equitably amongst us, so that none might have reason to complain. I"
1727 "then said that we had better make off at once, but my men very"
1727 "foolishly would not obey me, so they staid there drinking much wine and"
1727 "killing great numbers of sheep and oxen on the sea shore. Meanwhile the"
1727 "Cicons cried out for help to other Cicons who lived inland. These were"
1727 "more in number, and stronger, and they were more skilled in the art of"
1727 "war, for they could fight, either from chariots or on foot as the"
1727 "occasion served; in the morning, therefore, they came as thick as"
1727 "leaves and bloom in summer, and the hand of heaven was against us, so"
1727 "that we were hard pressed. They set the battle in array near the ships,"
1727 "and the hosts aimed their bronze-shod spears at one another.76 So long"
1727 "as the day waxed and it was still morning, we held our own against"
1727 "them, though they were more in number than we; but as the sun went"
1727 "down, towards the time when men loose their oxen, the Cicons got the"
1727 "better of us, and we lost half a dozen men from every ship we had; so"
1727 "we got away with those that were left."
1727 ""
1727 "“Thence we sailed onward with sorrow in our hearts, but glad to have"
1727 "escaped death though we had lost our comrades, nor did we leave till we"
1727 "had thrice invoked each one of the poor fellows who had perished by the"
1727 "hands of the Cicons. Then Jove raised the North wind against us till it"
1727 "blew a hurricane, so that land and sky were hidden in thick clouds, and"
1727 "night sprang forth out of the heavens. We let the ships run before the"
1727 "gale, but the force of the wind tore our sails to tatters, so we took"
1727 "them down for fear of shipwreck, and rowed our hardest towards the"
1727 "land. There we lay two days and two nights suffering much alike from"
1727 "toil and distress of mind, but on the morning of the third day we again"
1727 "raised our masts, set sail, and took our places, letting the wind and"
1727 "steersmen direct our ship. I should have got home at that time unharmed"
1727 "had not the North wind and the currents been against me as I was"
1727 "doubling Cape Malea, and set me off my course hard by the island of"
1727 "Cythera."
1727 ""
1727 "“I was driven thence by foul winds for a space of nine days upon the"
1727 "sea, but on the tenth day we reached the land of the Lotus-eaters, who"
1727 "live on a food that comes from a kind of flower. Here we landed to take"
1727 "in fresh water, and our crews got their mid-day meal on the shore near"
1727 "the ships. When they had eaten and drunk I sent two of my company to"
1727 "see what manner of men the people of the place might be, and they had a"
1727 "third man under them. They started at once, and went about among the"
1727 "Lotus-eaters, who did them no hurt, but gave them to eat of the lotus,"
1727 "which was so delicious that those who ate of it left off caring about"
1727 "home, and did not even want to go back and say what had happened to"
1727 "them, but were for staying and munching lotus77 with the Lotus-eaters"
1727 "without thinking further of their return; nevertheless, though they"
1727 "wept bitterly I forced them back to the ships and made them fast under"
1727 "the benches. Then I told the rest to go on board at once, lest any of"
1727 "them should taste of the lotus and leave off wanting to get home, so"
1727 "they took their places and smote the grey sea with their oars."
1727 ""
1727 "“We sailed hence, always in much distress, till we came to the land of"
1727 "the lawless and inhuman Cyclopes. Now the Cyclopes neither plant nor"
1727 "plough, but trust in providence, and live on such wheat, barley, and"
1727 "grapes as grow wild without any kind of tillage, and their wild grapes"
1727 "yield them wine as the sun and the rain may grow them. They have no"
1727 "laws nor assemblies of the people, but live in caves on the tops of"
1727 "high mountains; each is lord and master in his family, and they take no"
1727 "account of their neighbours."
1727 ""
1727 "“Now off their harbour there lies a wooded and fertile island not quite"
1727 "close to the land of the Cyclopes, but still not far. It is over-run"
1727 "with wild goats, that breed there in great numbers and are never"
1727 "disturbed by foot of man; for sportsmen—who as a rule will suffer so"
1727 "much hardship in forest or among mountain precipices—do not go there,"
1727 "nor yet again is it ever ploughed or fed down, but it lies a wilderness"
1727 "untilled and unsown from year to year, and has no living thing upon it"
1727 "but only goats. For the Cyclopes have no ships, nor yet shipwrights who"
1727 "could make ships for them; they cannot therefore go from city to city,"
1727 "or sail over the sea to one another’s country as people who have ships"
1727 "can do; if they had had these they would have colonised the island,78"
1727 "for it is a very good one, and would yield everything in due season."
1727 "There are meadows that in some places come right down to the sea shore,"
1727 "well watered and full of luscious grass; grapes would do there"
1727 "excellently; there is level land for ploughing, and it would always"
1727 "yield heavily at harvest time, for the soil is deep. There is a good"
1727 "harbour where no cables are wanted, nor yet anchors, nor need a ship be"
1727 "moored, but all one has to do is to beach one’s vessel and stay there"
1727 "till the wind becomes fair for putting out to sea again. At the head of"
1727 "the harbour there is a spring of clear water coming out of a cave, and"
1727 "there are poplars growing all round it."
1727 ""
1727 "“Here we entered, but so dark was the night that some god must have"
1727 "brought us in, for there was nothing whatever to be seen. A thick mist"
1727 "hung all round our ships;79 the moon was hidden behind a mass of clouds"
1727 "so that no one could have seen the island if he had looked for it, nor"
1727 "were there any breakers to tell us we were close in shore before we"
1727 "found ourselves upon the land itself; when, however, we had beached the"
1727 "ships, we took down the sails, went ashore and camped upon the beach"
1727 "till daybreak."
1727 ""
1727 "“When the child of morning, rosy-fingered Dawn appeared, we admired the"
1727 "island and wandered all over it, while the nymphs Jove’s daughters"
1727 "roused the wild goats that we might get some meat for our dinner. On"
1727 "this we fetched our spears and bows and arrows from the ships, and"
1727 "dividing ourselves into three bands began to shoot the goats. Heaven"
1727 "sent us excellent sport; I had twelve ships with me, and each ship got"
1727 "nine goats, while my own ship had ten; thus through the livelong day to"
1727 "the going down of the sun we ate and drank our fill, and we had plenty"
1727 "of wine left, for each one of us had taken many jars full when we"
1727 "sacked the city of the Cicons, and this had not yet run out. While we"
1727 "were feasting we kept turning our eyes towards the land of the"
1727 "Cyclopes, which was hard by, and saw the smoke of their stubble fires."
1727 "We could almost fancy we heard their voices and the bleating of their"
1727 "sheep and goats, but when the sun went down and it came on dark, we"
1727 "camped down upon the beach, and next morning I called a council."
1727 ""
1727 "“‘Stay here, my brave fellows,’ said I, ‘all the rest of you, while I"
1727 "go with my ship and exploit these people myself: I want to see if they"
1727 "are uncivilised savages, or a hospitable and humane race.’"
1727 ""
1727 "“I went on board, bidding my men to do so also and loose the hawsers;"
1727 "so they took their places and smote the grey sea with their oars. When"
1727 "we got to the land, which was not far, there, on the face of a cliff"
1727 "near the sea, we saw a great cave overhung with laurels. It was a"
1727 "station for a great many sheep and goats, and outside there was a large"
1727 "yard, with a high wall round it made of stones built into the ground"
1727 "and of trees both pine and oak. This was the abode of a huge monster"
1727 "who was then away from home shepherding his flocks. He would have"
1727 "nothing to do with other people, but led the life of an outlaw. He was"
1727 "a horrid creature, not like a human being at all, but resembling rather"
1727 "some crag that stands out boldly against the sky on the top of a high"
1727 "mountain."
1727 ""
1727 "“I told my men to draw the ship ashore, and stay where they were, all"
1727 "but the twelve best among them, who were to go along with myself. I"
1727 "also took a goatskin of sweet black wine which had been given me by"
1727 "Maron, son of Euanthes, who was priest of Apollo the patron god of"
1727 "Ismarus, and lived within the wooded precincts of the temple. When we"
1727 "were sacking the city we respected him, and spared his life, as also"
1727 "his wife and child; so he made me some presents of great value—seven"
1727 "talents of fine gold, and a bowl of silver, with twelve jars of sweet"
1727 "wine, unblended, and of the most exquisite flavour. Not a man nor maid"
1727 "in the house knew about it, but only himself, his wife, and one"
1727 "housekeeper: when he drank it he mixed twenty parts of water to one of"
1727 "wine, and yet the fragrance from the mixing-bowl was so exquisite that"
1727 "it was impossible to refrain from drinking. I filled a large skin with"
1727 "this wine, and took a wallet full of provisions with me, for my mind"
1727 "misgave me that I might have to deal with some savage who would be of"
1727 "great strength, and would respect neither right nor law."
1727 ""
1727 "“We soon reached his cave, but he was out shepherding, so we went"
1727 "inside and took stock of all that we could see. His cheese-racks were"
1727 "loaded with cheeses, and he had more lambs and kids than his pens could"
1727 "hold. They were kept in separate flocks; first there were the hoggets,"
1727 "then the oldest of the younger lambs and lastly the very young ones80"
1727 "all kept apart from one another; as for his dairy, all the vessels,"
1727 "bowls, and milk pails into which he milked, were swimming with whey."
1727 "When they saw all this, my men begged me to let them first steal some"
1727 "cheeses, and make off with them to the ship; they would then return,"
1727 "drive down the lambs and kids, put them on board and sail away with"
1727 "them. It would have been indeed better if we had done so but I would"
1727 "not listen to them, for I wanted to see the owner himself, in the hope"
1727 "that he might give me a present. When, however, we saw him my poor men"
1727 "found him ill to deal with."
1727 ""
1727 "“We lit a fire, offered some of the cheeses in sacrifice, ate others of"
1727 "them, and then sat waiting till the Cyclops should come in with his"
1727 "sheep. When he came, he brought in with him a huge load of dry firewood"
1727 "to light the fire for his supper, and this he flung with such a noise"
1727 "on to the floor of his cave that we hid ourselves for fear at the far"
1727 "end of the cavern. Meanwhile he drove all the ewes inside, as well as"
1727 "the she-goats that he was going to milk, leaving the males, both rams"
1727 "and he-goats, outside in the yards. Then he rolled a huge stone to the"
1727 "mouth of the cave—so huge that two and twenty strong four-wheeled"
1727 "waggons would not be enough to draw it from its place against the"
1727 "doorway. When he had so done he sat down and milked his ewes and goats,"
1727 "all in due course, and then let each of them have her own young. He"
1727 "curdled half the milk and set it aside in wicker strainers, but the"
1727 "other half he poured into bowls that he might drink it for his supper."
1727 "When he had got through with all his work, he lit the fire, and then"
1727 "caught sight of us, whereon he said:"
1727 ""
1727 "“‘Strangers, who are you? Where do sail from? Are you traders, or do"
1727 "you sail the sea as rovers, with your hands against every man, and"
1727 "every man’s hand against you?’"
1727 ""
1727 "“We were frightened out of our senses by his loud voice and monstrous"
1727 "form, but I managed to say, ‘We are Achaeans on our way home from Troy,"
1727 "but by the will of Jove, and stress of weather, we have been driven far"
1727 "out of our course. We are the people of Agamemnon, son of Atreus, who"
1727 "has won infinite renown throughout the whole world, by sacking so great"
1727 "a city and killing so many people. We therefore humbly pray you to show"
1727 "us some hospitality, and otherwise make us such presents as visitors"
1727 "may reasonably expect. May your excellency fear the wrath of heaven,"
1727 "for we are your suppliants, and Jove takes all respectable travellers"
1727 "under his protection, for he is the avenger of all suppliants and"
1727 "foreigners in distress.’"
1727 ""
1727 "“To this he gave me but a pitiless answer, ‘Stranger,’ said he, ‘you"
1727 "are a fool, or else you know nothing of this country. Talk to me,"
1727 "indeed, about fearing the gods or shunning their anger? We Cyclopes do"
1727 "not care about Jove or any of your blessed gods, for we are ever so"
1727 "much stronger than they. I shall not spare either yourself or your"
1727 "companions out of any regard for Jove, unless I am in the humour for"
1727 "doing so. And now tell me where you made your ship fast when you came"
1727 "on shore. Was it round the point, or is she lying straight off the"
1727 "land?’"
1727 ""
1727 "“He said this to draw me out, but I was too cunning to be caught in"
1727 "that way, so I answered with a lie; ‘Neptune,’ said I, ‘sent my ship on"
1727 "to the rocks at the far end of your country, and wrecked it. We were"
1727 "driven on to them from the open sea, but I and those who are with me"
1727 "escaped the jaws of death.’"
1727 ""
1727 "“The cruel wretch vouchsafed me not one word of answer, but with a"
1727 "sudden clutch he gripped up two of my men at once and dashed them down"
1727 "upon the ground as though they had been puppies. Their brains were shed"
1727 "upon the ground, and the earth was wet with their blood. Then he tore"
1727 "them limb from limb and supped upon them. He gobbled them up like a"
1727 "lion in the wilderness, flesh, bones, marrow, and entrails, without"
1727 "leaving anything uneaten. As for us, we wept and lifted up our hands to"
1727 "heaven on seeing such a horrid sight, for we did not know what else to"
1727 "do; but when the Cyclops had filled his huge paunch, and had washed"
1727 "down his meal of human flesh with a drink of neat milk, he stretched"
1727 "himself full length upon the ground among his sheep, and went to sleep."
1727 "I was at first inclined to seize my sword, draw it, and drive it into"
1727 "his vitals, but I reflected that if I did we should all certainly be"
1727 "lost, for we should never be able to shift the stone which the monster"
1727 "had put in front of the door. So we stayed sobbing and sighing where we"
1727 "were till morning came."
1727 ""
1727 "“When the child of morning, rosy-fingered dawn, appeared, he again lit"
1727 "his fire, milked his goats and ewes, all quite rightly, and then let"
1727 "each have her own young one; as soon as he had got through with all his"
1727 "work, he clutched up two more of my men, and began eating them for his"
1727 "morning’s meal. Presently, with the utmost ease, he rolled the stone"
1727 "away from the door and drove out his sheep, but he at once put it back"
1727 "again—as easily as though he were merely clapping the lid on to a"
1727 "quiver full of arrows. As soon as he had done so he shouted, and cried"
1727 "‘Shoo, shoo,’ after his sheep to drive them on to the mountain; so I"
1727 "was left to scheme some way of taking my revenge and covering myself"
1727 "with glory."
1727 ""
1727 "“In the end I deemed it would be the best plan to do as follows: The"
1727 "Cyclops had a great club which was lying near one of the sheep pens; it"
1727 "was of green olive wood, and he had cut it intending to use it for a"
1727 "staff as soon as it should be dry. It was so huge that we could only"
1727 "compare it to the mast of a twenty-oared merchant vessel of large"
1727 "burden, and able to venture out into open sea. I went up to this club"
1727 "and cut off about six feet of it; I then gave this piece to the men and"
1727 "told them to fine it evenly off at one end, which they proceeded to do,"
1727 "and lastly I brought it to a point myself, charring the end in the fire"
1727 "to make it harder. When I had done this I hid it under dung, which was"
1727 "lying about all over the cave, and told the men to cast lots which of"
1727 "them should venture along with myself to lift it and bore it into the"
1727 "monster’s eye while he was asleep. The lot fell upon the very four whom"
1727 "I should have chosen, and I myself made five. In the evening the wretch"
1727 "came back from shepherding, and drove his flocks into the cave—this"
1727 "time driving them all inside, and not leaving any in the yards; I"
1727 "suppose some fancy must have taken him, or a god must have prompted him"
1727 "to do so. As soon as he had put the stone back to its place against the"
1727 "door, he sat down, milked his ewes and his goats all quite rightly, and"
1727 "then let each have her own young one; when he had got through with all"
1727 "this work, he gripped up two more of my men, and made his supper off"
1727 "them. So I went up to him with an ivy-wood bowl of black wine in my"
1727 "hands:"
1727 ""
1727 "“‘Look here, Cyclops,’ said I, you have been eating a great deal of"
1727 "man’s flesh, so take this and drink some wine, that you may see what"
1727 "kind of liquor we had on board my ship. I was bringing it to you as a"
1727 "drink-offering, in the hope that you would take compassion upon me and"
1727 "further me on my way home, whereas all you do is to go on ramping and"
1727 "raving most intolerably. You ought to be ashamed of yourself; how can"
1727 "you expect people to come see you any more if you treat them in this"
1727 "way?’"
1727 ""
1727 "“He then took the cup and drank. He was so delighted with the taste of"
1727 "the wine that he begged me for another bowl full. ‘Be so kind,’ he"
1727 "said, ‘as to give me some more, and tell me your name at once. I want"
1727 "to make you a present that you will be glad to have. We have wine even"
1727 "in this country, for our soil grows grapes and the sun ripens them, but"
1727 "this drinks like Nectar and Ambrosia all in one.’"
1727 ""
1727 "“I then gave him some more; three times did I fill the bowl for him,"
1727 "and three times did he drain it without thought or heed; then, when I"
1727 "saw that the wine had got into his head, I said to him as plausibly as"
1727 "I could: ‘Cyclops, you ask my name and I will tell it you; give me,"
1727 "therefore, the present you promised me; my name is Noman; this is what"
1727 "my father and mother and my friends have always called me.’"
1727 ""
1727 "“But the cruel wretch said, ‘Then I will eat all Noman’s comrades"
1727 "before Noman himself, and will keep Noman for the last. This is the"
1727 "present that I will make him.’"
1727 ""
1727 "“As he spoke he reeled, and fell sprawling face upwards on the ground."
1727 "His great neck hung heavily backwards and a deep sleep took hold upon"
1727 "him. Presently he turned sick, and threw up both wine and the gobbets"
1727 "of human flesh on which he had been gorging, for he was very drunk."
1727 "Then I thrust the beam of wood far into the embers to heat it, and"
1727 "encouraged my men lest any of them should turn faint-hearted. When the"
1727 "wood, green though it was, was about to blaze, I drew it out of the"
1727 "fire glowing with heat, and my men gathered round me, for heaven had"
1727 "filled their hearts with courage. We drove the sharp end of the beam"
1727 "into the monster’s eye, and bearing upon it with all my weight I kept"
1727 "turning it round and round as though I were boring a hole in a ship’s"
1727 "plank with an auger, which two men with a wheel and strap can keep on"
1727 "turning as long as they choose. Even thus did we bore the red hot beam"
1727 "into his eye, till the boiling blood bubbled all over it as we worked"
1727 "it round and round, so that the steam from the burning eyeball scalded"
1727 "his eyelids and eyebrows, and the roots of the eye sputtered in the"
1727 "fire. As a blacksmith plunges an axe or hatchet into cold water to"
1727 "temper it—for it is this that gives strength to the iron—and it makes a"
1727 "great hiss as he does so, even thus did the Cyclops’ eye hiss round the"
1727 "beam of olive wood, and his hideous yells made the cave ring again. We"
1727 "ran away in a fright, but he plucked the beam all besmirched with gore"
1727 "from his eye, and hurled it from him in a frenzy of rage and pain,"
1727 "shouting as he did so to the other Cyclopes who lived on the bleak"
1727 "headlands near him; so they gathered from all quarters round his cave"
1727 "when they heard him crying, and asked what was the matter with him."
1727 ""
1727 "“‘What ails you, Polyphemus,’ said they, ‘that you make such a noise,"
1727 "breaking the stillness of the night, and preventing us from being able"
1727 "to sleep? Surely no man is carrying off your sheep? Surely no man is"
1727 "trying to kill you either by fraud or by force?’"
1727 ""
1727 "“But Polyphemus shouted to them from inside the cave, ‘Noman is killing"
1727 "me by fraud; no man is killing me by force.’"
1727 ""
1727 "“‘Then,’ said they, ‘if no man is attacking you, you must be ill; when"
1727 "Jove makes people ill, there is no help for it, and you had better pray"
1727 "to your father Neptune.’"
1727 ""
1727 "“Then they went away, and I laughed inwardly at the success of my"
1727 "clever stratagem, but the Cyclops, groaning and in an agony of pain,"

1727 "door; then he sat in the doorway and stretched his hands in front of it"

1727 "foolish enough to attempt this."
1727 ""
1727 "“As for myself I kept on puzzling to think how I could best save my own"

1727 "knows that his life depends upon it, for the danger was very great. In"

1727 "well grown, and carried a heavy black fleece, so I bound them"

1727 "the thick wool under his belly, and hung on patiently to his fleece,"
1727 "face upwards, keeping a firm hold on it all the time."
1727 ""
1727 "“Thus, then, did we wait in great fear of mind till morning came, but"

1727 "underneath their bellies. As the ram was going out, last of all, heavy"

1727 "hold of it and said:"
1727 ""

1727 ""
1727 "“As he spoke he drove the ram outside, but when we were a little way"

1727 ""

1727 ""
1727 "“He got more and more furious as he heard me, so he tore the top from"

1727 ""

1727 "excellent seer, and did all the prophesying for the Cyclopes till he"

1727 ""
1727 "“Then I said, ‘I wish I could be as sure of killing you outright and"

1727 ""

1727 ""

1727 "AEOLUS, THE LAESTRYGONES, CIRCE."
1727 ""
1727 ""
1727 "“Thence we went on to the Aeolian island where lives Aeolus son of"
1727 "Hippotas, dear to the immortal gods. It is an island that floats (as it"
1727 "were) upon the sea,83 iron bound with a wall that girds it. Now, Aeolus"
1727 "has six daughters and six lusty sons, so he made the sons marry the"
1727 "daughters, and they all live with their dear father and mother,"
1727 "feasting and enjoying every conceivable kind of luxury. All day long"
1727 "the atmosphere of the house is loaded with the savour of roasting meats"
1727 "till it groans again, yard and all; but by night they sleep on their"
1727 "well made bedsteads, each with his own wife between the blankets. These"
1727 "were the people among whom we had now come."
1727 ""
1727 "“Aeolus entertained me for a whole month asking me questions all the"
1727 "time about Troy, the Argive fleet, and the return of the Achaeans. I"
1727 "told him exactly how everything had happened, and when I said I must"
1727 "go, and asked him to further me on my way, he made no sort of"
1727 "difficulty, but set about doing so at once. Moreover, he flayed me a"
1727 "prime ox-hide to hold the ways of the roaring winds, which he shut up"
1727 "in the hide as in a sack—for Jove had made him captain over the winds,"
1727 "and he could stir or still each one of them according to his own"
1727 "pleasure. He put the sack in the ship and bound the mouth so tightly"
1727 "with a silver thread that not even a breath of a side-wind could blow"
1727 "from any quarter. The West wind which was fair for us did he alone let"
1727 "blow as it chose; but it all came to nothing, for we were lost through"
1727 "our own folly."
1727 ""
1727 "“Nine days and nine nights did we sail, and on the tenth day our native"
1727 "land showed on the horizon. We got so close in that we could see the"
1727 "stubble fires burning, and I, being then dead beat, fell into a light"
1727 "sleep, for I had never let the rudder out of my own hands, that we"
1727 "might get home the faster. On this the men fell to talking among"
1727 "themselves, and said I was bringing back gold and silver in the sack"
1727 "that Aeolus had given me. ‘Bless my heart,’ would one turn to his"
1727 "neighbour, saying, ‘how this man gets honoured and makes friends to"
1727 "whatever city or country he may go. See what fine prizes he is taking"
1727 "home from Troy, while we, who have travelled just as far as he has,"
1727 "come back with hands as empty as we set out with—and now Aeolus has"
1727 "given him ever so much more. Quick—let us see what it all is, and how"
1727 "much gold and silver there is in the sack he gave him.’"
1727 ""
1727 "“Thus they talked and evil counsels prevailed. They loosed the sack,"
1727 "whereupon the wind flew howling forth and raised a storm that carried"
1727 "us weeping out to sea and away from our own country. Then I awoke, and"
1727 "knew not whether to throw myself into the sea or to live on and make"
1727 "the best of it; but I bore it, covered myself up, and lay down in the"
1727 "ship, while the men lamented bitterly as the fierce winds bore our"
1727 "fleet back to the Aeolian island."
1727 ""
1727 "“When we reached it we went ashore to take in water, and dined hard by"
1727 "the ships. Immediately after dinner I took a herald and one of my men"
1727 "and went straight to the house of Aeolus, where I found him feasting"
1727 "with his wife and family; so we sat down as suppliants on the"
1727 "threshold. They were astounded when they saw us and said, ‘Ulysses,"
1727 "what brings you here? What god has been ill-treating you? We took great"
1727 "pains to further you on your way home to Ithaca, or wherever it was"
1727 "that you wanted to go to.’"
1727 ""
1727 "“Thus did they speak, but I answered sorrowfully, ‘My men have undone"
1727 "me; they, and cruel sleep, have ruined me. My friends, mend me this"
1727 "mischief, for you can if you will.’"
1727 ""
1727 "“I spoke as movingly as I could, but they said nothing, till their"
1727 "father answered, ‘Vilest of mankind, get you gone at once out of the"
1727 "island; him whom heaven hates will I in no wise help. Be off, for you"
1727 "come here as one abhorred of heaven.’ And with these words he sent me"
1727 "sorrowing from his door."
1727 ""
1727 "“Thence we sailed sadly on till the men were worn out with long and"
1727 "fruitless rowing, for there was no longer any wind to help them. Six"
1727 "days, night and day did we toil, and on the seventh day we reached the"
1727 "rocky stronghold of Lamus—Telepylus, the city of the Laestrygonians,"
1727 "where the shepherd who is driving in his sheep and goats [to be milked]"
1727 "salutes him who is driving out his flock [to feed] and this last"
1727 "answers the salute. In that country a man who could do without sleep"
1727 "might earn double wages, one as a herdsman of cattle, and another as a"
1727 "shepherd, for they work much the same by night as they do by day.84"
1727 ""
1727 "“When we reached the harbour we found it land-locked under steep"
1727 "cliffs, with a narrow entrance between two headlands. My captains took"
1727 "all their ships inside, and made them fast close to one another, for"
1727 "there was never so much as a breath of wind inside, but it was always"
1727 "dead calm. I kept my own ship outside, and moored it to a rock at the"
1727 "very end of the point; then I climbed a high rock to reconnoitre, but"
1727 "could see no sign neither of man nor cattle, only some smoke rising"
1727 "from the ground. So I sent two of my company with an attendant to find"
1727 "out what sort of people the inhabitants were."
1727 ""
1727 "“The men when they got on shore followed a level road by which the"
1727 "people draw their firewood from the mountains into the town, till"
1727 "presently they met a young woman who had come outside to fetch water,"
1727 "and who was daughter to a Laestrygonian named Antiphates. She was going"
1727 "to the fountain Artacia from which the people bring in their water, and"
1727 "when my men had come close up to her, they asked her who the king of"
1727 "that country might be, and over what kind of people he ruled; so she"
1727 "directed them to her father’s house, but when they got there they found"
1727 "his wife to be a giantess as huge as a mountain, and they were"
1727 "horrified at the sight of her."
1727 ""
1727 "“She at once called her husband Antiphates from the place of assembly,"
1727 "and forthwith he set about killing my men. He snatched up one of them,"
1727 "and began to make his dinner off him then and there, whereon the other"
1727 "two ran back to the ships as fast as ever they could. But Antiphates"
1727 "raised a hue-and-cry after them, and thousands of sturdy Laestrygonians"
1727 "sprang up from every quarter—ogres, not men. They threw vast rocks at"
1727 "us from the cliffs as though they had been mere stones, and I heard the"
1727 "horrid sound of the ships crunching up against one another, and the"
1727 "death cries of my men, as the Laestrygonians speared them like fishes"
1727 "and took them home to eat them. While they were thus killing my men"
1727 "within the harbour I drew my sword, cut the cable of my own ship, and"
1727 "told my men to row with all their might if they too would not fare like"
1727 "the rest; so they laid out for their lives, and we were thankful enough"
1727 "when we got into open water out of reach of the rocks they hurled at"
1727 "us. As for the others there was not one of them left."
1727 ""
1727 "“Thence we sailed sadly on, glad to have escaped death, though we had"
1727 "lost our comrades, and came to the Aeaean island, where Circe lives—a"
1727 "great and cunning goddess who is own sister to the magician Aeetes—for"
1727 "they are both children of the sun by Perse, who is daughter to Oceanus."
1727 "We brought our ship into a safe harbour without a word, for some god"
1727 "guided us thither, and having landed we lay there for two days and two"
1727 "nights, worn out in body and mind. When the morning of the third day"
1727 "came I took my spear and my sword, and went away from the ship to"
1727 "reconnoitre, and see if I could discover signs of human handiwork, or"
1727 "hear the sound of voices. Climbing to the top of a high look-out I"
1727 "espied the smoke of Circe’s house rising upwards amid a dense forest of"
1727 "trees, and when I saw this I doubted whether, having seen the smoke, I"
1727 "would not go on at once and find out more, but in the end I deemed it"
1727 "best to go back to the ship, give the men their dinners, and send some"
1727 "of them instead of going myself."
1727 ""
1727 "“When I had nearly got back to the ship some god took pity upon my"
1727 "solitude, and sent a fine antlered stag right into the middle of my"
1727 "path. He was coming down his pasture in the forest to drink of the"
1727 "river, for the heat of the sun drove him, and as he passed I struck him"
1727 "in the middle of the back; the bronze point of the spear went clean"
1727 "through him, and he lay groaning in the dust until the life went out of"
1727 "him. Then I set my foot upon him, drew my spear from the wound, and"
1727 "laid it down; I also gathered rough grass and rushes and twisted them"
1727 "into a fathom or so of good stout rope, with which I bound the four"
1727 "feet of the noble creature together; having so done I hung him round my"
1727 "neck and walked back to the ship leaning upon my spear, for the stag"
1727 "was much too big for me to be able to carry him on my shoulder,"
1727 "steadying him with one hand. As I threw him down in front of the ship,"
1727 "I called the men and spoke cheeringly man by man to each of them. ‘Look"
1727 "here my friends,’ said I, ‘we are not going to die so much before our"
1727 "time after all, and at any rate we will not starve so long as we have"
1727 "got something to eat and drink on board.’ On this they uncovered their"
1727 "heads upon the sea shore and admired the stag, for he was indeed a"
1727 "splendid fellow. Then, when they had feasted their eyes upon him"
1727 "sufficiently, they washed their hands and began to cook him for dinner."
1727 ""
1727 "“Thus through the livelong day to the going down of the sun we stayed"
1727 "there eating and drinking our fill, but when the sun went down and it"
1727 "came on dark, we camped upon the sea shore. When the child of morning,"
1727 "rosy-fingered Dawn, appeared, I called a council and said, ‘My friends,"
1727 "we are in very great difficulties; listen therefore to me. We have no"
1727 "idea where the sun either sets or rises,85 so that we do not even know"
1727 "East from West. I see no way out of it; nevertheless, we must try and"
1727 "find one. We are certainly on an island, for I went as high as I could"
1727 "this morning, and saw the sea reaching all round it to the horizon; it"
1727 "lies low, but towards the middle I saw smoke rising from out of a thick"
1727 "forest of trees.’"
1727 ""
1727 "“Their hearts sank as they heard me, for they remembered how they had"
1727 "been treated by the Laestrygonian Antiphates, and by the savage ogre"
1727 "Polyphemus. They wept bitterly in their dismay, but there was nothing"
1727 "to be got by crying, so I divided them into two companies and set a"
1727 "captain over each; I gave one company to Eurylochus, while I took"
1727 "command of the other myself. Then we cast lots in a helmet, and the lot"
1727 "fell upon Eurylochus; so he set out with his twenty-two men, and they"
1727 "wept, as also did we who were left behind."
1727 ""
1727 "“When they reached Circe’s house they found it built of cut stones, on"
1727 "a site that could be seen from far, in the middle of the forest. There"
1727 "were wild mountain wolves and lions prowling all round it—poor"
1727 "bewitched creatures whom she had tamed by her enchantments and drugged"
1727 "into subjection. They did not attack my men, but wagged their great"
1727 "tails, fawned upon them, and rubbed their noses lovingly against"
1727 "them.86 As hounds crowd round their master when they see him coming"
1727 "from dinner—for they know he will bring them something—even so did"
1727 "these wolves and lions with their great claws fawn upon my men, but the"
1727 "men were terribly frightened at seeing such strange creatures."
1727 "Presently they reached the gates of the goddess’s house, and as they"
1727 "stood there they could hear Circe within, singing most beautifully as"
1727 "she worked at her loom, making a web so fine, so soft, and of such"
1727 "dazzling colours as no one but a goddess could weave. On this Polites,"
1727 "whom I valued and trusted more than any other of my men, said, ‘There"
1727 "is some one inside working at a loom and singing most beautifully; the"
1727 "whole place resounds with it, let us call her and see whether she is"
1727 "woman or goddess.’"
1727 ""
1727 "“They called her and she came down, unfastened the door, and bade them"
1727 "enter. They, thinking no evil, followed her, all except Eurylochus, who"
1727 "suspected mischief and staid outside. When she had got them into her"
1727 "house, she set them upon benches and seats and mixed them a mess with"
1727 "cheese, honey, meal, and Pramnian wine, but she drugged it with wicked"
1727 "poisons to make them forget their homes, and when they had drunk she"
1727 "turned them into pigs by a stroke of her wand, and shut them up in her"
1727 "pig-styes. They were like pigs—head, hair, and all, and they grunted"
1727 "just as pigs do; but their senses were the same as before, and they"
1727 "remembered everything."
1727 ""
1727 "“Thus then were they shut up squealing, and Circe threw them some"
1727 "acorns and beech masts such as pigs eat, but Eurylochus hurried back to"
1727 "tell me about the sad fate of our comrades. He was so overcome with"
1727 "dismay that though he tried to speak he could find no words to do so;"
1727 "his eyes filled with tears and he could only sob and sigh, till at last"
1727 "we forced his story out of him, and he told us what had happened to the"
1727 "others."
1727 ""
1727 "“‘We went,’ said he, ‘as you told us, through the forest, and in the"
1727 "middle of it there was a fine house built with cut stones in a place"
1727 "that could be seen from far. There we found a woman, or else she was a"
1727 "goddess, working at her loom and singing sweetly; so the men shouted to"
1727 "her and called her, whereon she at once came down, opened the door, and"

1727 "be some treachery. From that moment I saw them no more, for not one of"
1727 "them ever came out, though I sat a long time watching for them.’"
1727 ""
1727 "“Then I took my sword of bronze and slung it over my shoulders; I also"

1727 "way. But he laid hold of me with both his hands and spoke piteously,"

1727 "“‘Stay where you are, then,’ answered I, ‘eating and drinking at the"

1727 ""
1727 "“With this I left the ship and went up inland. When I got through the"

1727 ""
1727 "“‘And I will tell you of all the wicked witchcraft that Circe will try"
1727 "to practice upon you. She will mix a mess for you to drink, and she"
1727 "will drug the meal with which she makes it, but she will not be able to"
1727 "charm you, for the virtue of the herb that I shall give you will"
1727 "prevent her spells from working. I will tell you all about it. When"

1727 "refuse her, for you want her to set your companions free, and to take"
1727 "good care also of yourself, but you must make her swear solemnly by all"

1727 ""

1727 "can do whatever they like."

1727 "“Then, when we had got down to the sea shore we drew our ship into the"
1727 "water and got her mast and sails into her; we also put the sheep on"
1727 "board and took our places, weeping and in great distress of mind."
1727 "Circe, that great and cunning goddess, sent us a fair wind that blew"
1727 "dead aft and staid steadily with us keeping our sails all the time well"
1727 "filled; so we did whatever wanted doing to the ship’s gear and let her"
1727 "go as the wind and helmsman headed her. All day long her sails were"
1727 "full as she held her course over the sea, but when the sun went down"
1727 "and darkness was over all the earth, we got into the deep waters of the"
1727 "river Oceanus, where lie the land and city of the Cimmerians who live"
1727 "enshrouded in mist and darkness which the rays of the sun never pierce"
1727 "neither at his rising nor as he goes down again out of the heavens, but"
1727 "the poor wretches live in one long melancholy night. When we got there"
1727 "we beached the ship, took the sheep out of her, and went along by the"
1727 "waters of Oceanus till we came to the place of which Circe had told us."
1727 ""
1727 "“Here Perimedes and Eurylochus held the victims, while I drew my sword"
1727 "and dug the trench a cubit each way. I made a drink-offering to all the"
1727 "dead, first with honey and milk, then with wine, and thirdly with"
1727 "water, and I sprinkled white barley meal over the whole, praying"
1727 "earnestly to the poor feckless ghosts, and promising them that when I"
1727 "got back to Ithaca I would sacrifice a barren heifer for them, the best"
1727 "I had, and would load the pyre with good things. I also particularly"
1727 "promised that Teiresias should have a black sheep to himself, the best"
1727 "in all my flocks. When I had prayed sufficiently to the dead, I cut the"
1727 "throats of the two sheep and let the blood run into the trench, whereon"
1727 "the ghosts came trooping up from Erebus—brides,89 young bachelors, old"
1727 "men worn out with toil, maids who had been crossed in love, and brave"
1727 "men who had been killed in battle, with their armour still smirched"
1727 "with blood; they came from every quarter and flitted round the trench"
1727 "with a strange kind of screaming sound that made me turn pale with"
1727 "fear. When I saw them coming I told the men to be quick and flay the"
1727 "carcasses of the two dead sheep and make burnt offerings of them, and"
1727 "at the same time to repeat prayers to Hades and to Proserpine; but I"
1727 "sat where I was with my sword drawn and would not let the poor feckless"
1727 "ghosts come near the blood till Teiresias should have answered my"
1727 "questions."
1727 ""

1727 "grieves continually about your never having come home, and suffers more"
1727 "and more as he grows older. As for my own end it was in this wise:"
1727 "heaven did not take me swiftly and painlessly in my own house, nor was"
1727 "I attacked by any illness such as those that generally wear people out"
1727 "and kill them, but my longing to know what you were doing and the force"
1727 "of my affection for you—this it was that was the death of me.’93"
1727 ""
1727 "“Then I tried to find some way of embracing my poor mother’s ghost."
1727 "Thrice I sprang towards her and tried to clasp her in my arms, but each"
1727 "time she flitted from my embrace as it were a dream or phantom, and"
1727 "being touched to the quick I said to her, ‘Mother, why do you not stay"
1727 "still when I would embrace you? If we could throw our arms around one"
1727 "another we might find sad comfort in the sharing of our sorrows even in"
1727 "the house of Hades; does Proserpine want to lay a still further load of"
1727 "grief upon me by mocking me with a phantom only?’"
1727 ""
1727 "“‘My son,’ she answered, ‘most ill-fated of all mankind, it is not"

1727 "they are dead. The sinews no longer hold the flesh and bones together;"
1727 "these perish in the fierceness of consuming fire as soon as life has"

1727 "these things that you may tell them to your wife hereafter.’"
1727 ""
1727 "“Thus did we converse, and anon Proserpine sent up the ghosts of the"

1727 "her told me her race and lineage."
1727 ""
1727 "“The first I saw was Tyro. She was daughter of Salmoneus and wife of"

1727 "THE SIRENS, SCYLLA AND CHARYBDIS, THE CATTLE OF THE SUN."

1727 ""
1727 "“After we were clear of the river Oceanus, and had got out into the"
1727 "open sea, we went on till we reached the Aeaean island where there is"
1727 "dawn and sun-rise as in other places. We then drew our ship on to the"
1727 "sands and got out of her on to the shore, where we went to sleep and"
1727 "waited till day should break."
1727 ""
1727 "“Then, when the child of morning, rosy-fingered Dawn, appeared, I sent"
1727 "some men to Circe’s house to fetch the body of Elpenor. We cut firewood"
1727 "from a wood where the headland jutted out into the sea, and after we"
1727 "had wept over him and lamented him we performed his funeral rites. When"
1727 "his body and armour had been burned to ashes, we raised a cairn, set a"
1727 "stone over it, and at the top of the cairn we fixed the oar that he had"
1727 "been used to row with."
1727 ""
1727 "“While we were doing all this, Circe, who knew that we had got back"
1727 "from the house of Hades, dressed herself and came to us as fast as she"
1727 "could; and her maid servants came with her bringing us bread, meat, and"
1727 "wine. Then she stood in the midst of us and said, ‘You have done a bold"
1727 "thing in going down alive to the house of Hades, and you will have died"
1727 "twice, to other people’s once; now, then, stay here for the rest of the"
1727 "day, feast your fill, and go on with your voyage at daybreak tomorrow"
1727 "morning. In the meantime I will tell Ulysses about your course, and"
1727 "will explain everything to him so as to prevent your suffering from"
1727 "misadventure either by land or sea.’"
1727 ""
1727 "“We agreed to do as she had said, and feasted through the livelong day"
1727 "to the going down of the sun, but when the sun had set and it came on"
1727 "dark, the men laid themselves down to sleep by the stern cables of the"
1727 "ship. Then Circe took me by the hand and bade me be seated away from"
1727 "the others, while she reclined by my side and asked me all about our"
1727 "adventures."
1727 ""
1727 "“‘So far so good,’ said she, when I had ended my story, ‘and now pay"
1727 "attention to what I am about to tell you—heaven itself, indeed, will"
1727 "recall it to your recollection. First you will come to the Sirens who"
1727 "enchant all who come near them. If any one unwarily draws in too close"
1727 "and hears the singing of the Sirens, his wife and children will never"
1727 "welcome him home again, for they sit in a green field and warble him to"
1727 "death with the sweetness of their song. There is a great heap of dead"
1727 "men’s bones lying all around, with the flesh still rotting off them."
1727 "Therefore pass these Sirens by, and stop your men’s ears with wax that"
1727 "none of them may hear; but if you like you can listen yourself, for you"
1727 "may get the men to bind you as you stand upright on a cross piece half"
1727 "way up the mast,99 and they must lash the rope’s ends to the mast"
1727 "itself, that you may have the pleasure of listening. If you beg and"
1727 "pray the men to unloose you, then they must bind you faster."
1727 ""
1727 "“‘When your crew have taken you past these Sirens, I cannot give you"
1727 "coherent directions100 as to which of two courses you are to take; I"

1727 "to send another to make up their number; no ship that ever yet came to"
1727 "these rocks has got away again, but the waves and whirlwinds of fire"
1727 "are freighted with wreckage and with the bodies of dead men. The only"
1727 "vessel that ever sailed and got through, was the famous Argo on her way"

1727 "great rocks, only that Juno piloted her past them for the love she bore"

1727 ""
1727 "“‘Of these two rocks the one reaches heaven and its peak is lost in a"
1727 "dark cloud. This never leaves it, so that the top is never clear not"

1727 "“‘You will find the other rock lie lower, but they are so close"

1727 "“‘You will now come to the Thrinacian island, and here you will see"

1727 "“Then, being much troubled in mind, I said to my men, ‘My friends, it"

1727 "“When we had passed the [Wandering] rocks, with Scylla and terrible"

1727 ""
1727 "“The men were in despair at this, and Eurylochus at once gave me an"

1727 ""

1727 "Thus did he speak, and they all held their peace throughout the covered"
1727 "cloister, enthralled by the charm of his story, till presently Alcinous"
1727 "began to speak."
1727 ""
1727 "“Ulysses,” said he, “now that you have reached my house I doubt not you"
1727 "will get home without further misadventure no matter how much you have"
1727 "suffered in the past. To you others, however, who come here night after"
1727 "night to drink my choicest wine and listen to my bard, I would insist"
1727 "as follows. Our guest has already packed up the clothes, wrought"
1727 "gold,108 and other valuables which you have brought for his acceptance;"
1727 "let us now, therefore, present him further, each one of us, with a"
1727 "large tripod and a cauldron. We will recoup ourselves by the levy of a"
1727 "general rate; for private individuals cannot be expected to bear the"
1727 "burden of such a handsome present.”"
1727 ""
1727 "Every one approved of this, and then they went home to bed each in his"
1727 "own abode. When the child of morning, rosy-fingered Dawn, appeared they"
1727 "hurried down to the ship and brought their cauldrons with them."
1727 "Alcinous went on board and saw everything so securely stowed under the"
1727 "ship’s benches that nothing could break adrift and injure the rowers."
1727 "Then they went to the house of Alcinous to get dinner, and he"
1727 "sacrificed a bull for them in honour of Jove who is the lord of all."
1727 "They set the steaks to grill and made an excellent dinner, after which"
1727 "the inspired bard, Demodocus, who was a favourite with every one, sang"
1727 "to them; but Ulysses kept on turning his eyes towards the sun, as"
1727 "though to hasten his setting, for he was longing to be on his way. As"
1727 "one who has been all day ploughing a fallow field with a couple of oxen"
1727 "keeps thinking about his supper and is glad when night comes that he"
1727 "may go and get it, for it is all his legs can do to carry him, even so"
1727 "did Ulysses rejoice when the sun went down, and he at once said to the"
1727 "Phaeacians, addressing himself more particularly to King Alcinous:"
1727 ""
1727 "“Sir, and all of you, farewell. Make your drink-offerings and send me"
1727 "on my way rejoicing, for you have fulfilled my heart’s desire by giving"
1727 "me an escort, and making me presents, which heaven grant that I may"
1727 "turn to good account; may I find my admirable wife living in peace"
1727 "among friends,109 and may you whom I leave behind me give satisfaction"
1727 "to your wives and children;110 may heaven vouchsafe you every good"
1727 "grace, and may no evil thing come among your people.”"
1727 ""

1727 "agreed that he should have his escort inasmuch as he had spoken"
1727 "reasonably. Alcinous therefore said to his servant, “Pontonous, mix"

1727 "father Jove, and speed our guest upon his way.”"
1727 ""

1727 ""

1727 ""
1727 "When the bright star that heralds the approach of dawn began to show,"

1727 "ULYSSES IN THE HUT WITH EUMAEUS."
1727 ""
1727 ""
1727 "Ulysses now left the haven, and took the rough track up through the"
1727 "wooded country and over the crest of the mountain till he reached the"
1727 "place where Minerva had said that he would find the swineherd, who was"
1727 "the most thrifty servant he had. He found him sitting in front of his"
1727 "hut, which was by the yards that he had built on a site which could be"
1727 "seen from far. He had made them spacious126 and fair to see, with a"
1727 "free run for the pigs all round them; he had built them during his"
1727 "master’s absence, of stones which he had gathered out of the ground,"
1727 "without saying anything to Penelope or Laertes, and he had fenced them"
1727 "on top with thorn bushes. Outside the yard he had run a strong fence of"
1727 "oaken posts, split, and set pretty close together, while inside he had"
1727 "built twelve styes near one another for the sows to lie in. There were"
1727 "fifty pigs wallowing in each stye, all of them breeding sows; but the"
1727 "boars slept outside and were much fewer in number, for the suitors kept"
1727 "on eating them, and the swineherd had to send them the best he had"
1727 "continually. There were three hundred and sixty boar pigs, and the"
1727 "herdsman’s four hounds, which were as fierce as wolves, slept always"
1727 "with them. The swineherd was at that moment cutting out a pair of"
1727 "sandals127 from a good stout ox hide. Three of his men were out herding"
1727 "the pigs in one place or another, and he had sent the fourth to town"
1727 "with a boar that he had been forced to send the suitors that they might"
1727 "sacrifice it and have their fill of meat."
1727 ""
1727 "When the hounds saw Ulysses they set up a furious barking and flew at"
1727 "him, but Ulysses was cunning enough to sit down and loose his hold of"
1727 "the stick that he had in his hand: still, he would have been torn by"
1727 "them in his own homestead had not the swineherd dropped his ox hide,"
1727 "rushed full speed through the gate of the yard and driven the dogs off"
1727 "by shouting and throwing stones at them. Then he said to Ulysses, “Old"
1727 "man, the dogs were likely to have made short work of you, and then you"
1727 "would have got me into trouble. The gods have given me quite enough"
1727 "worries without that, for I have lost the best of masters, and am in"
1727 "continual grief on his account. I have to attend swine for other people"
1727 "to eat, while he, if he yet lives to see the light of day, is starving"
1727 "in some distant land. But come inside, and when you have had your fill"
1727 "of bread and wine, tell me where you come from, and all about your"
1727 "misfortunes.”"
1727 ""
1727 "On this the swineherd led the way into the hut and bade him sit down."
1727 "He strewed a good thick bed of rushes upon the floor, and on the top of"
1727 "this he threw the shaggy chamois skin—a great thick one—on which he"
1727 "used to sleep by night. Ulysses was pleased at being made thus welcome,"
1727 "and said “May Jove, sir, and the rest of the gods grant you your"
1727 "heart’s desire in return for the kind way in which you have received"
1727 "me.”"
1727 ""
1727 "To this you answered, O swineherd Eumaeus, “Stranger, though a still"
1727 "poorer man should come here, it would not be right for me to insult"
1727 "him, for all strangers and beggars are from Jove. You must take what"
1727 "you can get and be thankful, for servants live in fear when they have"
1727 "young lords for their masters; and this is my misfortune now, for"
1727 "heaven has hindered the return of him who would have been always good"
1727 "to me and given me something of my own—a house, a piece of land, a good"
1727 "looking wife, and all else that a liberal master allows a servant who"
1727 "has worked hard for him, and whose labour the gods have prospered as"
1727 "they have mine in the situation which I hold. If my master had grown"
1727 "old here he would have done great things by me, but he is gone, and I"
1727 "wish that Helen’s whole race were utterly destroyed, for she has been"
1727 "the death of many a good man. It was this matter that took my master to"
1727 "Ilius, the land of noble steeds, to fight the Trojans in the cause of"
1727 "king Agamemnon.”"
1727 ""
1727 "As he spoke he bound his girdle round him and went to the styes where"

1727 "“Fall to, stranger,” said he, “on a dish of servant’s pork. The fat"
1727 "pigs have to go to the suitors, who eat them up without shame or"
1727 "scruple; but the blessed gods love not such shameful doings, and"
1727 "respect those who do what is lawful and right. Even the fierce"

1727 "and sending it to them.”"

1727 "MINERVA SUMMONS TELEMACHUS FROM LACEDAEMON—HE MEETS WITH THEOCLYMENUS"
1727 "AT PYLOS AND BRINGS HIM TO ITHACA—ON LANDING HE GOES TO THE HUT OF"

1727 "But Minerva went to the fair city of Lacedaemon to tell Ulysses’ son"

1727 "Ulysses answered, “Eumaeus, I have heard the story of your misfortunes"
1727 "with the most lively interest and pity, but Jove has given you good as"
1727 "well as evil, for in spite of everything you have a good master, who"
1727 "sees that you always have enough to eat and drink; and you lead a good"
1727 "life, whereas I am still going about begging my way from city to city.”"
1727 ""
1727 "Thus did they converse, and they had only a very little time left for"
1727 "sleep, for it was soon daybreak. In the mean time Telemachus and his"
1727 "crew were nearing land, so they loosed the sails, took down the mast,"
1727 "and rowed the ship into the harbour.136 They cast out their mooring"
1727 "stones and made fast the hawsers; they then got out upon the sea shore,"
1727 "mixed their wine, and got dinner ready. As soon as they had had enough"
1727 "to eat and drink Telemachus said, “Take the ship on to the town, but"
1727 "leave me here, for I want to look after the herdsmen on one of my"
1727 "farms. In the evening, when I have seen all I want, I will come down to"
1727 "the city, and to-morrow morning in return for your trouble I will give"
1727 "you all a good dinner with meat and wine.” 137"
1727 ""
1727 "Then Theoclymenus said, “And what, my dear young friend, is to become"
1727 "of me? To whose house, among all your chief men, am I to repair? or"
1727 "shall I go straight to your own house and to your mother?”"
1727 ""
1727 "“At any other time,” replied Telemachus, “I should have bidden you go"
1727 "to my own house, for you would find no want of hospitality; at the"
1727 "present moment, however, you would not be comfortable there, for I"
1727 "shall be away, and my mother will not see you; she does not often show"
1727 "herself even to the suitors, but sits at her loom weaving in an upper"
1727 "chamber, out of their way; but I can tell you a man whose house you can"
1727 "go to—I mean Eurymachus the son of Polybus, who is held in the highest"
1727 "estimation by every one in Ithaca. He is much the best man and the most"
1727 "persistent wooer, of all those who are paying court to my mother and"
1727 "trying to take Ulysses’ place. Jove, however, in heaven alone knows"
1727 "whether or no they will come to a bad end before the marriage takes"
1727 "place.”"
1727 ""
1727 "As he was speaking a bird flew by upon his right hand—a hawk, Apollo’s"
1727 "messenger. It held a dove in its talons, and the feathers, as it tore"
1727 "them off,138 fell to the ground midway between Telemachus and the ship."
1727 "On this Theoclymenus called him apart and caught him by the hand."
1727 "“Telemachus,” said he, “that bird did not fly on your right hand"
1727 "without having been sent there by some god. As soon as I saw it I knew"

1727 "will be no house in Ithaca more royal than your own.”"
1727 ""
1727 "“I wish it may prove so,” answered Telemachus. “If it does, I will show"
1727 "you so much good will and give you so many presents that all who meet"
1727 "you will congratulate you.”"
1727 ""

1727 ""

1727 ""

1727 "Meanwhile Ulysses and the swineherd had lit a fire in the hut and were"
1727 "were getting breakfast ready at daybreak, for they had sent the men out"
1727 "with the pigs. When Telemachus came up, the dogs did not bark but"
1727 "fawned upon him, so Ulysses, hearing the sound of feet and noticing"
1727 "that the dogs did not bark, said to Eumaeus:"
1727 ""
1727 "“Eumaeus, I hear footsteps; I suppose one of your men or some one of"
1727 "your acquaintance is coming here, for the dogs are fawning upon him and"
1727 "not barking.”"
1727 ""
1727 "The words were hardly out of his mouth before his son stood at the"
1727 "door. Eumaeus sprang to his feet, and the bowls in which he was mixing"
1727 "wine fell from his hands, as he made towards his master. He kissed his"
1727 "head and both his beautiful eyes, and wept for joy. A father could not"
1727 "be more delighted at the return of an only son, the child of his old"
1727 "age, after ten years’ absence in a foreign country and after having"
1727 "gone through much hardship. He embraced him, kissed him all over as"
1727 "though he had come back from the dead, and spoke fondly to him saying:"
1727 ""

1727 "heard you had gone to Pylos I made sure I was never going to see you"
1727 "any more. Come in, my dear child, and sit down, that I may have a good"
1727 "look at you now you are home again; it is not very often you come into"
1727 "the country to see us herdsmen; you stick pretty close to the town"
1727 "generally. I suppose you think it better to keep an eye on what the"
1727 "suitors are doing.”"
1727 ""
1727 "“So be it, old friend,” answered Telemachus, “but I am come now because"
1727 "I want to see you, and to learn whether my mother is still at her old"
1727 "home or whether some one else has married her, so that the bed of"
1727 "Ulysses is without bedding and covered with cobwebs.”"
1727 ""

1727 ""
1727 "As he spoke he took Telemachus’ spear, whereon he crossed the stone"
1727 "threshold and came inside. Ulysses rose from his seat to give him place"

1727 "he, “I can easily find another seat, and there is one here who will lay"
1727 "it for me.”"
1727 ""

1727 ""
1727 "“I am very much distressed,” said Telemachus, “by what you have just"

1727 "Then Ulysses said, “Sir, it is right that I should say something"

1727 ""
1727 "Thus did they converse, and meanwhile the ship which had brought"
1727 "Telemachus and his crew from Pylos had reached the town of Ithaca. When"
1727 "they had come inside the harbour they drew the ship on to the land;"
1727 "their servants came and took their armour from them, and they left all"
1727 "the presents at the house of Clytius. Then they sent a servant to tell"

1727 "ship to the town to prevent her from being alarmed and made unhappy."
1727 "This servant and Eumaeus happened to meet when they were both on the"

1727 "servant stood up and said to the queen in the presence of the waiting"

1727 "close up to Penelope, and said privately all that her son had bidden"

1727 "outbuildings and went back to his pigs again."
1727 ""
1727 "The suitors were surprised and angry at what had happened, so they went"
1727 "outside the great wall that ran round the outer court, and held a"
1727 "council near the main entrance. Eurymachus, son of Polybus, was the"
1727 "first to speak."
1727 ""
1727 "“My friends,” said he, “this voyage of Telemachus’s is a very serious"
1727 "matter; we had made sure that it would come to nothing. Now, however,"
1727 "let us draw a ship into the water, and get a crew together to send"
1727 "after the others and tell them to come back as fast as they can.”"
1727 ""
1727 "He had hardly done speaking when Amphinomus turned in his place and saw"
1727 "the ship inside the harbour, with the crew lowering her sails, and"
1727 "putting by their oars; so he laughed, and said to the others, “We need"
1727 "not send them any message, for they are here. Some god must have told"
1727 "them, or else they saw the ship go by, and could not overtake her.”"
1727 ""
1727 "On this they rose and went to the water side. The crew then drew the"
1727 "ship on shore; their servants took their armour from them, and they"
1727 "went up in a body to the place of assembly, but they would not let any"
1727 "one old or young sit along with them, and Antinous, son of Eupeithes,"
1727 "spoke first."
1727 ""
1727 "“Good heavens,” said he, “see how the gods have saved this man from"
1727 "destruction. We kept a succession of scouts upon the headlands all day"

1727 "in doing so, for he will be furious with us, and will tell all the"
1727 "world how we plotted to kill him, but failed to take him. The people"
1727 "will not like this when they come to know of it; we must see that they"

1727 "mother and the man who marries her have the house. If this does not"

1727 ""
1727 "They all held their peace until Amphinomus rose to speak. He was the"
1727 "son of Nisus, who was son to king Aretias, and he was foremost among"
1727 "all the suitors from the wheat-growing and well grassed island of"

1727 ""

1727 "TELEMACHUS AND HIS MOTHER MEET—ULYSSES AND EUMAEUS COME DOWN TO THE"
1727 "TOWN, AND ULYSSES IS INSULTED BY MELANTHIUS—HE IS RECOGNISED BY THE DOG"
1727 "ARGOS—HE IS INSULTED AND PRESENTLY STRUCK BY ANTINOUS WITH A"
1727 "STOOL—PENELOPE DESIRES THAT HE SHALL BE SENT TO HER."
1727 ""
1727 ""
1727 "When the child of morning, rosy-fingered Dawn, appeared, Telemachus"
1727 "bound on his sandals and took a strong spear that suited his hands, for"
1727 "he wanted to go into the city. “Old friend,” said he to the swineherd,"
1727 "“I will now go to the town and show myself to my mother, for she will"
1727 "never leave off grieving till she has seen me. As for this unfortunate"
1727 "stranger, take him to the town and let him beg there of any one who"
1727 "will give him a drink and a piece of bread. I have trouble enough of my"
1727 "own, and cannot be burdened with other people. If this makes him angry"
1727 "so much the worse for him, but I like to say what I mean.”"
1727 ""
1727 "Then Ulysses said, “Sir, I do not want to stay here; a beggar can"
1727 "always do better in town than country, for any one who likes can give"
1727 "him something. I am too old to care about remaining here at the beck"
1727 "and call of a master. Therefore let this man do as you have just told"
1727 "him, and take me to the town as soon as I have had a warm by the fire,"
1727 "and the day has got a little heat in it. My clothes are wretchedly"
1727 "thin, and this frosty morning I shall be perished with cold, for you"
1727 "say the city is some way off.”"
1727 ""
1727 "On this Telemachus strode off through the yards, brooding his revenge"
1727 "upon the suitors. When he reached home he stood his spear against a"
1727 "bearing-post of the cloister, crossed the stone floor of the cloister"
1727 "itself, and went inside."
1727 ""
1727 "Nurse Euryclea saw him long before any one else did. She was putting"
1727 "the fleeces on to the seats, and she burst out crying as she ran up to"
1727 "him; all the other maids came up too, and covered his head and"
1727 "shoulders with their kisses. Penelope came out of her room looking like"

1727 "she cried as she spoke fondly to him, “so you are come home again; I"

1727 ""

1727 "suitors. I must now go to the place of assembly to invite a stranger"
1727 "who has come back with me from Pylos. I sent him on with my crew, and"
1727 "told Piraeus to take him home and look after him till I could come for"
1727 "him myself.”"
1727 ""
1727 "She heeded her son’s words, washed her face, changed her dress, and"

1727 ""

1727 ""
1727 "THE FIGHT WITH IRUS—ULYSSES WARNS AMPHINOMUS—PENELOPE GETS PRESENTS"
1727 "FROM THE SUITORS—THE BRAZIERS—ULYSSES REBUKES EURYMACHUS."
1727 ""
1727 ""
1727 "Now there came a certain common tramp who used to go begging all over"
1727 "the city of Ithaca, and was notorious as an incorrigible glutton and"
1727 "drunkard. This man had no strength nor stay in him, but he was a great"
1727 "hulking fellow to look at; his real name, the one his mother gave him,"
1727 "was Arnaeus, but the young men of the place called him Irus,148 because"
1727 "he used to run errands for any one who would send him. As soon as he"
1727 "came he began to insult Ulysses, and to try and drive him out of his"
1727 "own house."
1727 ""
1727 "“Be off, old man,” he cried, “from the doorway, or you shall be dragged"
1727 "out neck and heels. Do you not see that they are all giving me the"
1727 "wink, and wanting me to turn you out by force, only I do not like to do"
1727 "so? Get up then, and go of yourself, or we shall come to blows.”"
1727 ""
1727 "Ulysses frowned on him and said, “My friend, I do you no manner of"
1727 "harm; people give you a great deal, but I am not jealous. There is room"
1727 "enough in this doorway for the pair of us, and you need not grudge me"
1727 "things that are not yours to give. You seem to be just such another"
1727 "tramp as myself, but perhaps the gods will give us better luck by and"
1727 "by. Do not, however, talk too much about fighting or you will incense"
1727 "me, and old though I am, I shall cover your mouth and chest with blood."
1727 "I shall have more peace tomorrow if I do, for you will not come to the"
1727 "house of Ulysses any more.”"
1727 ""
1727 "Irus was very angry and answered, “You filthy glutton, you run on"
1727 "trippingly like an old fish-fag. I have a good mind to lay both hands"
1727 "about you, and knock your teeth out of your head like so many boar’s"
1727 "tusks. Get ready, therefore, and let these people here stand by and"
1727 "look on. You will never be able to fight one who is so much younger"
1727 "than yourself.”"
1727 ""
1727 "Thus roundly did they rate one another on the smooth pavement in front"
1727 "of the doorway,149 and when Antinous saw what was going on he laughed"
1727 "heartily and said to the others, “This is the finest sport that you"
1727 "ever saw; heaven never yet sent anything like it into this house. The"
1727 "stranger and Irus have quarreled and are going to fight, let us set"
1727 "them on to do so at once.”"
1727 ""
1727 "The suitors all came up laughing, and gathered round the two ragged"
1727 "tramps. “Listen to me,” said Antinous, “there are some goats’ paunches"
1727 "down at the fire, which we have filled with blood and fat, and set"
1727 "aside for supper; he who is victorious and proves himself to be the"
1727 "better man shall have his pick of the lot; he shall be free of our"
1727 "table and we will not allow any other beggar about the house at all.”"
1727 ""
1727 "The others all agreed, but Ulysses, to throw them off the scent, said,"
1727 "“Sirs, an old man like myself, worn out with suffering, cannot hold his"
1727 "own against a young one; but my irrepressible belly urges me on, though"
1727 "I know it can only end in my getting a drubbing. You must swear,"
1727 "however that none of you will give me a foul blow to favour Irus and"
1727 "secure him the victory.”"
1727 ""
1727 "They swore as he told them, and when they had completed their oath"
1727 "Telemachus put in a word and said, “Stranger, if you have a mind to"
1727 "settle with this fellow, you need not be afraid of any one here."
1727 "Whoever strikes you will have to fight more than one. I am host, and"
1727 "the other chiefs, Antinous and Eurymachus, both of them men of"
1727 "understanding, are of the same mind as I am.”"
1727 ""
1727 "Every one assented, and Ulysses girded his old rags about his loins,"
1727 "thus baring his stalwart thighs, his broad chest and shoulders, and his"
1727 "mighty arms; but Minerva came up to him and made his limbs even"
1727 "stronger still. The suitors were beyond measure astonished, and one"
1727 "would turn towards his neighbour saying, “The stranger has brought such"
1727 "a thigh out of his old rags that there will soon be nothing left of"
1727 "Irus.”"
1727 ""
1727 "Irus began to be very uneasy as he heard them, but the servants girded"
1727 "him by force, and brought him [into the open part of the court] in such"
1727 "a fright that his limbs were all of a tremble. Antinous scolded him and"
1727 "said, “You swaggering bully, you ought never to have been born at all"
1727 "if you are afraid of such an old broken down creature as this tramp is."
1727 "I say, therefore—and it shall surely be—if he beats you and proves"
1727 "himself the better man, I shall pack you off on board ship to the"
1727 "mainland and send you to king Echetus, who kills every one that comes"
1727 "near him. He will cut off your nose and ears, and draw out your"
1727 "entrails for the dogs to eat.”"
1727 ""
1727 "This frightened Irus still more, but they brought him into the middle"
1727 "of the court, and the two men raised their hands to fight. Then Ulysses"
1727 "considered whether he should let drive so hard at him as to make an end"
1727 "of him then and there, or whether he should give him a lighter blow"
1727 "that should only knock him down; in the end he deemed it best to give"
1727 "the lighter blow for fear the Achaeans should begin to suspect who he"
1727 "was. Then they began to fight, and Irus hit Ulysses on the right"
1727 "shoulder; but Ulysses gave Irus a blow on the neck under the ear that"
1727 "broke in the bones of his skull, and the blood came gushing out of his"
1727 "mouth; he fell groaning in the dust, gnashing his teeth and kicking on"
1727 "the ground, but the suitors threw up their hands and nearly died of"
1727 "laughter, as Ulysses caught hold of him by the foot and dragged him"
1727 "into the outer court as far as the gate-house. There he propped him up"
1727 "against the wall and put his staff in his hands. “Sit here,” said he,"
1727 "“and keep the dogs and pigs off; you are a pitiful creature, and if you"
1727 "try to make yourself king of the beggars any more you shall fare still"
1727 "worse.”"
1727 ""
1727 "Then he threw his dirty old wallet, all tattered and torn over his"
1727 "shoulder with the cord by which it hung, and went back to sit down upon"
1727 "the threshold; but the suitors went within the cloisters, laughing and"
1727 "saluting him, “May Jove, and all the other gods,” said they, “grant you"

1727 "insatiable tramp. We will take him over to the mainland presently, to"
1727 "king Echetus, who kills every one that comes near him.”"
1727 ""
1727 "Ulysses hailed this as of good omen, and Antinous set a great goat’s"
1727 "paunch before him filled with blood and fat. Amphinomus took two loaves"
1727 "out of the bread-basket and brought them to him, pledging him as he did"

1727 ""
1727 "To this Ulysses answered, “Amphinomus, you seem to be a man of good"
1727 "understanding, as indeed you may well be, seeing whose son you are. I"
1727 "have heard your father well spoken of; he is Nisus of Dulichium, a man"

1727 "saying. Man is the vainest of all creatures that have their being upon"
1727 "earth. As long as heaven vouchsafes him health and strength, he thinks"
1727 "that he shall come to no harm hereafter, and even when the blessed gods"
1727 "bring sorrow upon him, he bears it as he needs must, and makes the best"

1727 ""

1727 ""

1727 ""
1727 "With these words she came down from her upper room, not alone but"

1727 ""
1727 "“Telemachus,” said she, addressing her son, “I fear you are no longer"
1727 "so discreet and well conducted as you used to be. When you were younger"
1727 "you had a greater sense of propriety; now, however, that you are grown"
