1727 "Minerva, and Apollo would break the neck of every one of these wooers"

1727 "Ulysses was left in the cloister, pondering on the means whereby with"
1727 "Minerva’s help he might be able to kill the suitors. Presently he said"
1727 "to Telemachus, “Telemachus, we must get the armour together and take it"
1727 "down inside. Make some excuse when the suitors ask you why you have"
1727 "removed it. Say that you have taken it to be out of the way of the"
1727 "smoke, inasmuch as it is no longer what it was when Ulysses went away,"

1727 "particularly that you are afraid Jove may set them on to quarrel over"
1727 "their wine, and that they may do each other some harm which may"
1727 "disgrace both banquet and wooing, for the sight of arms sometimes"
1727 "tempts people to use them.”"
1727 ""
1727 "Telemachus approved of what his father had said, so he called nurse"
1727 "Euryclea and said, “Nurse, shut the women up in their room, while I"

1727 "room. No one looks after it now my father is gone, and it has got all"
1727 "smirched with soot during my own boyhood. I want to take it down where"
1727 "the smoke cannot reach it.”"
1727 ""

1727 "Ulysses scowled at her and answered, “My good woman, why should you be"

1727 "Then Penelope answered, “Stranger, heaven robbed me of all beauty,"

1727 "Then Ulysses answered, “Madam, wife of Ulysses, since you persist in"

1727 ""
1727 "Many a plausible tale did Ulysses further tell her, and Penelope wept"
1727 "as she listened, for her heart was melted. As the snow wastes upon the"
1727 "mountain tops when the winds from South East and West have breathed"
1727 "upon it and thawed it till the rivers run bank full with water, even so"
1727 "did her cheeks overflow with tears for the husband who was all the time"

1727 "relieved herself by weeping, she turned to him again and said: “Now,"
1727 "stranger, I shall put you to the test and see whether or no you really"
1727 "did entertain my husband and his men, as you say you did. Tell me,"
1727 "then, how he was dressed, what kind of a man he was to look at, and so"
1727 "also with his companions.”"
1727 ""
1727 "“Madam,” answered Ulysses, “it is such a long time ago that I can"
1727 "hardly say. Twenty years are come and gone since he left my home, and"
1727 "went elsewhither; but I will tell you as well as I can recollect."
1727 "Ulysses wore a mantle of purple wool, double lined, and it was fastened"
1727 "by a gold brooch with two catches for the pin. On the face of this"
1727 "there was a device that shewed a dog holding a spotted fawn between his"
1727 "fore paws, and watching it as it lay panting upon the ground. Every one"
1727 "marvelled at the way in which these things had been done in gold, the"
1727 "dog looking at the fawn, and strangling it, while the fawn was"
1727 "struggling convulsively to escape.153 As for the shirt that he wore"
1727 "next his skin, it was so soft that it fitted him like the skin of an"
1727 "onion, and glistened in the sunlight to the admiration of all the women"
1727 "who beheld it. Furthermore I say, and lay my saying to your heart, that"
1727 "I do not know whether Ulysses wore these clothes when he left home, or"
1727 "whether one of his companions had given them to him while he was on his"
1727 "voyage; or possibly some one at whose house he was staying made him a"
1727 "present of them, for he was a man of many friends and had few equals"
1727 "among the Achaeans. I myself gave him a sword of bronze and a beautiful"
1727 "purple mantle, double lined, with a shirt that went down to his feet,"
1727 "and I sent him on board his ship with every mark of honour. He had a"
1727 "servant with him, a little older than himself, and I can tell you what"
1727 "he was like; his shoulders were hunched,154 he was dark, and he had"
1727 "thick curly hair. His name was Eurybates, and Ulysses treated him with"
1727 "greater familiarity than he did any of the others, as being the most"
1727 "like-minded with himself.”"
1727 ""
1727 "Penelope was moved still more deeply as she heard the indisputable"

1727 "detested city whose very name I cannot bring myself even to mention.”"
1727 ""
1727 "Then Ulysses answered, “Madam, wife of Ulysses, do not disfigure"
1727 "yourself further by grieving thus bitterly for your loss, though I can"
1727 "hardly blame you for doing so. A woman who has loved her husband and"
1727 "borne him children, would naturally be grieved at losing him, even"

1727 "ULYSSES CANNOT SLEEP—PENELOPE’S PRAYER TO DIANA—THE TWO SIGNS FROM"
1727 "HEAVEN—EUMAEUS AND PHILOETIUS ARRIVE—THE SUITORS DINE—CTESIPPUS THROWS"
1727 "AN OX’S FOOT AT ULYSSES—THEOCLYMENUS FORETELLS DISASTER AND LEAVES THE"
1727 "HOUSE."
1727 ""
1727 ""
1727 "Ulysses slept in the cloister upon an undressed bullock’s hide, on the"
1727 "top of which he threw several skins of the sheep the suitors had eaten,"
1727 "and Eurynome156 threw a cloak over him after he had laid himself down."
1727 "There, then, Ulysses lay wakefully brooding upon the way in which he"
1727 "should kill the suitors; and by and by, the women who had been in the"
1727 "habit of misconducting themselves with them, left the house giggling"
1727 "and laughing with one another. This made Ulysses very angry, and he"
1727 "doubted whether to get up and kill every single one of them then and"
1727 "there, or to let them sleep one more and last time with the suitors."
1727 "His heart growled within him, and as a bitch with puppies growls and"
1727 "shows her teeth when she sees a stranger, so did his heart growl with"
1727 "anger at the evil deeds that were being done: but he beat his breast"
1727 "and said, “Heart, be still, you had worse than this to bear on the day"
1727 "when the terrible Cyclops ate your brave companions; yet you bore it in"
1727 "silence till your cunning got you safe out of the cave, though you made"
1727 "sure of being killed.”"
1727 ""
1727 "Thus he chided with his heart, and checked it into endurance, but he"
1727 "tossed about as one who turns a paunch full of blood and fat in front"
1727 "of a hot fire, doing it first on one side and then on the other, that"
1727 "he may get it cooked as soon as possible, even so did he turn himself"
1727 "about from side to side, thinking all the time how, single handed as he"
1727 "was, he should contrive to kill so large a body of men as the wicked"
1727 "suitors. But by and by Minerva came down from heaven in the likeness of"
1727 "a woman, and hovered over his head saying, “My poor unhappy man, why do"
1727 "you lie awake in this way? This is your house: your wife is safe inside"
1727 "it, and so is your son who is just such a young man as any father may"
1727 "be proud of.”"
1727 ""
1727 "“Goddess,” answered Ulysses, “all that you have said is true, but I am"
1727 "in some doubt as to how I shall be able to kill these wicked suitors"
1727 "single handed, seeing what a number of them there always are. And there"
1727 "is this further difficulty, which is still more considerable. Supposing"
1727 "that with Jove’s and your assistance I succeed in killing them, I must"
1727 "ask you to consider where I am to escape to from their avengers when it"
1727 "is all over.”"
1727 ""
1727 "“For shame,” replied Minerva, “why, any one else would trust a worse"
1727 "ally than myself, even though that ally were only a mortal and less"
1727 "wise than I am. Am I not a goddess, and have I not protected you"
1727 "throughout in all your troubles? I tell you plainly that even though"
1727 "there were fifty bands of men surrounding us and eager to kill us, you"
1727 "should take all their sheep and cattle, and drive them away with you."
1727 "But go to sleep; it is a very bad thing to lie awake all night, and you"
1727 "shall be out of your troubles before long.”"
1727 ""
1727 "As she spoke she shed sleep over his eyes, and then went back to"
1727 "Olympus."
1727 ""
1727 "While Ulysses was thus yielding himself to a very deep slumber that"
1727 "eased the burden of his sorrows, his admirable wife awoke, and sitting"
1727 "up in her bed began to cry. When she had relieved herself by weeping"
1727 "she prayed to Diana saying, “Great Goddess Diana, daughter of Jove,"
1727 "drive an arrow into my heart and slay me; or let some whirlwind snatch"
1727 "me up and bear me through paths of darkness till it drop me into the"
1727 "mouths of over-flowing Oceanus, as it did the daughters of Pandareus."
1727 "The daughters of Pandareus lost their father and mother, for the gods"
1727 "killed them, so they were left orphans. But Venus took care of them,"
1727 "and fed them on cheese, honey, and sweet wine. Juno taught them to"
1727 "excel all women in beauty of form and understanding; Diana gave them an"
1727 "imposing presence, and Minerva endowed them with every kind of"
1727 "accomplishment; but one day when Venus had gone up to Olympus to see"
1727 "Jove about getting them married (for well does he know both what shall"
1727 "happen and what not happen to every one) the storm winds came and"
1727 "spirited them away to become handmaids to the dread Erinyes. Even so I"
1727 "wish that the gods who live in heaven would hide me from mortal sight,"
1727 "or that fair Diana might strike me, for I would fain go even beneath"
1727 "the sad earth if I might do so still looking towards Ulysses only, and"
1727 "without having to yield myself to a worse man than he was. Besides, no"
1727 "matter how much people may grieve by day, they can put up with it so"
1727 "long as they can sleep at night, for when the eyes are closed in"
1727 "slumber people forget good and ill alike; whereas my misery haunts me"
1727 "even in my dreams. This very night methought there was one lying by my"
1727 "side who was like Ulysses as he was when he went away with his host,"
1727 "and I rejoiced, for I believed that it was no dream, but the very truth"
1727 "itself.”"
1727 ""
1727 "On this the day broke, but Ulysses heard the sound of her weeping, and"
1727 "it puzzled him, for it seemed as though she already knew him and was by"
1727 "his side. Then he gathered up the cloak and the fleeces on which he had"
1727 "lain, and set them on a seat in the cloister, but he took the bullock’s"
1727 "hide out into the open. He lifted up his hands to heaven, and prayed,"
1727 "saying “Father Jove, since you have seen fit to bring me over land and"
1727 "sea to my own home after all the afflictions you have laid upon me,"
1727 "give me a sign out of the mouth of some one or other of those who are"
1727 "now waking within the house, and let me have another sign of some kind"
1727 "from outside.”"
1727 ""
1727 "Thus did he pray. Jove heard his prayer and forthwith thundered high up"
1727 "among the clouds from the splendour of Olympus, and Ulysses was glad"
1727 "when he heard it. At the same time within the house, a miller-woman"
1727 "from hard by in the mill room lifted up her voice and gave him another"
1727 "sign. There were twelve miller-women whose business it was to grind"
1727 "wheat and barley which are the staff of life. The others had ground"
1727 "their task and had gone to take their rest, but this one had not yet"
1727 "finished, for she was not so strong as they were, and when she heard"
1727 "the thunder she stopped grinding and gave the sign to her master."
1727 "“Father Jove,” said she, “you, who rule over heaven and earth, you have"
1727 "thundered from a clear sky without so much as a cloud in it, and this"
1727 "means something for somebody; grant the prayer, then, of me your poor"
1727 "servant who calls upon you, and let this be the very last day that the"
1727 "suitors dine in the house of Ulysses. They have worn me out with labour"
1727 "of grinding meal for them, and I hope they may never have another"
1727 "dinner anywhere at all.”"
1727 ""
1727 "Ulysses was glad when he heard the omens conveyed to him by the woman’s"
1727 "speech, and by the thunder, for he knew they meant that he should"
1727 "avenge himself on the suitors."

1727 "THE TRIAL OF THE AXES, DURING WHICH ULYSSES REVEALS HIMSELF TO EUMAEUS"
1727 "AND PHILOETIUS"
1727 ""
1727 ""
1727 "Minerva now put it in Penelope’s mind to make the suitors try their"
1727 "skill with the bow and with the iron axes, in contest among themselves,"
1727 "as a means of bringing about their destruction. She went upstairs and"
1727 "got the store-room key, which was made of bronze and had a handle of"
1727 "ivory; she then went with her maidens into the store-room at the end of"
1727 "the house, where her husband’s treasures of gold, bronze, and wrought"
1727 "iron were kept, and where was also his bow, and the quiver full of"
1727 "deadly arrows that had been given him by a friend whom he had met in"
1727 "Lacedaemon—Iphitus the son of Eurytus. The two fell in with one another"
1727 "in Messene at the house of Ortilochus, where Ulysses was staying in"
1727 "order to recover a debt that was owing from the whole people; for the"
1727 "Messenians had carried off three hundred sheep from Ithaca, and had"
1727 "sailed away with them and with their shepherds. In quest of these"
1727 "Ulysses took a long journey while still quite young, for his father and"
1727 "the other chieftains sent him on a mission to recover them. Iphitus had"
1727 "gone there also to try and get back twelve brood mares that he had"
1727 "lost, and the mule foals that were running with them. These mares were"
1727 "the death of him in the end, for when he went to the house of Jove’s"
1727 "son, mighty Hercules, who performed such prodigies of valour, Hercules"
1727 "to his shame killed him, though he was his guest, for he feared not"
1727 "heaven’s vengeance, nor yet respected his own table which he had set"
1727 "before Iphitus, but killed him in spite of everything, and kept the"
1727 "mares himself. It was when claiming these that Iphitus met Ulysses, and"
1727 "gave him the bow which mighty Eurytus had been used to carry, and which"
1727 "on his death had been left by him to his son. Ulysses gave him in"
1727 "return a sword and a spear, and this was the beginning of a fast"
1727 "friendship, although they never visited at one another’s houses, for"
1727 "Jove’s son Hercules killed Iphitus ere they could do so. This bow,"
1727 "then, given him by Iphitus, had not been taken with him by Ulysses when"
1727 "he sailed for Troy; he had used it so long as he had been at home, but"
1727 "had left it behind as having been a keepsake from a valued friend."
1727 ""
1727 "Penelope presently reached the oak threshold of the store-room; the"
1727 "carpenter had planed this duly, and had drawn a line on it so as to get"
1727 "it quite straight; he had then set the door posts into it and hung the"
1727 "doors. She loosed the strap from the handle of the door, put in the"
1727 "key, and drove it straight home to shoot back the bolts that held the"
1727 "doors;161 these flew open with a noise like a bull bellowing in a"
1727 "meadow, and Penelope stepped upon the raised platform, where the chests"
1727 "stood in which the fair linen and clothes were laid by along with"
1727 "fragrant herbs: reaching thence, she took down the bow with its bow"
1727 "case from the peg on which it hung. She sat down with it on her knees,"
1727 "weeping bitterly as she took the bow out of its case, and when her"
1727 "tears had relieved her, she went to the cloister where the suitors"
1727 "were, carrying the bow and the quiver, with the many deadly arrows that"
1727 "were inside it. Along with her came her maidens, bearing a chest that"
1727 "contained much iron and bronze which her husband had won as prizes."
1727 "When she reached the suitors, she stood by one of the bearing-posts"
1727 "supporting the roof of the cloister, holding a veil before her face,"
1727 "and with a maid on either side of her. Then she said:"
1727 ""
1727 "“Listen to me you suitors, who persist in abusing the hospitality of"
1727 "this house because its owner has been long absent, and without other"
1727 "pretext than that you want to marry me; this, then, being the prize"
1727 "that you are contending for, I will bring out the mighty bow of"
1727 "Ulysses, and whomsoever of you shall string it most easily and send his"
1727 "arrow through each one of twelve axes, him will I follow and quit this"
1727 "house of my lawful husband, so goodly, and so abounding in wealth. But"
1727 "even so I doubt not that I shall remember it in my dreams.”"
1727 ""
1727 "As she spoke, she told Eumaeus to set the bow and the pieces of iron"
1727 "before the suitors, and Eumaeus wept as he took them to do as she had"
1727 "bidden him. Hard by, the stockman wept also when he saw his master’s"
1727 "bow, but Antinous scolded them. “You country louts,” said he, “silly"
1727 "simpletons; why should you add to the sorrows of your mistress by"
1727 "crying in this way? She has enough to grieve her in the loss of her"
1727 "husband; sit still, therefore, and eat your dinners in silence, or go"
1727 "outside if you want to cry, and leave the bow behind you. We suitors"
1727 "shall have to contend for it with might and main, for we shall find it"
1727 "no light matter to string such a bow as this is. There is not a man of"
1727 "us all who is such another as Ulysses; for I have seen him and remember"
1727 "him, though I was then only a child.”"
1727 ""
1727 "This was what he said, but all the time he was expecting to be able to"
1727 "string the bow and shoot through the iron, whereas in fact he was to be"
1727 "the first that should taste of the arrows from the hands of Ulysses,"
1727 "whom he was dishonouring in his own house—egging the others on to do so"
1727 "also."
1727 ""
1727 "Then Telemachus spoke. “Great heavens!” he exclaimed, “Jove must have"
1727 "robbed me of my senses. Here is my dear and excellent mother saying she"
1727 "will quit this house and marry again, yet I am laughing and enjoying"
1727 "myself as though there were nothing happening. But, suitors, as the"
1727 "contest has been agreed upon, let it go forward. It is for a woman"
1727 "whose peer is not to be found in Pylos, Argos, or Mycene, nor yet in"
1727 "Ithaca nor on the mainland. You know this as well as I do; what need"
1727 "have I to speak in praise of my mother? Come on, then, make no excuses"
1727 "for delay, but let us see whether you can string the bow or no. I too"
1727 "will make trial of it, for if I can string it and shoot through the"
1727 "iron, I shall not suffer my mother to quit this house with a stranger,"
1727 "not if I can win the prizes which my father won before me.”"
1727 ""
1727 "As he spoke he sprang from his seat, threw his crimson cloak from him,"
1727 "and took his sword from his shoulder. First he set the axes in a row,"
1727 "in a long groove which he had dug for them, and had made straight by"
1727 "line.162 Then he stamped the earth tight round them, and everyone was"
1727 "surprised when they saw him set them up so orderly, though he had never"
1727 "seen anything of the kind before. This done, he went on to the pavement"
1727 "to make trial of the bow; thrice did he tug at it, trying with all his"
1727 "might to draw the string, and thrice he had to leave off, though he had"
1727 "hoped to string the bow and shoot through the iron. He was trying for"
1727 "the fourth time, and would have strung it had not Ulysses made a sign"
1727 "to check him in spite of all his eagerness. So he said:"
1727 ""
1727 "“Alas! I shall either be always feeble and of no prowess, or I am too"
1727 "young, and have not yet reached my full strength so as to be able to"
1727 "hold my own if any one attacks me. You others, therefore, who are"
1727 "stronger than I, make trial of the bow and get this contest settled.”"
1727 ""
1727 "On this he put the bow down, letting it lean against the door [that led"
1727 "into the house] with the arrow standing against the top of the bow."
1727 "Then he sat down on the seat from which he had risen, and Antinous"
1727 "said:"
1727 ""
1727 "“Come on each of you in his turn, going towards the right from the"
1727 "place at which the cupbearer begins when he is handing round the wine.”"
1727 ""
1727 "The rest agreed, and Leiodes son of Oenops was the first to rise. He"
1727 "was sacrificial priest to the suitors, and sat in the corner near the"
1727 "mixing-bowl. 163 He was the only man who hated their evil deeds and was"
1727 "indignant with the others. He was now the first to take the bow and"
1727 "arrow, so he went on to the pavement to make his trial, but he could"
1727 "not string the bow, for his hands were weak and unused to hard work,"
1727 "they therefore soon grew tired, and he said to the suitors, “My"
1727 "friends, I cannot string it; let another have it, this bow shall take"
1727 "the life and soul out of many a chief among us, for it is better to die"
1727 "than to live after having missed the prize that we have so long striven"
1727 "for, and which has brought us so long together. Some one of us is even"
1727 "now hoping and praying that he may marry Penelope, but when he has seen"
1727 "this bow and tried it, let him woo and make bridal offerings to some"
1727 "other woman, and let Penelope marry whoever makes her the best offer"
1727 "and whose lot it is to win her.”"
1727 ""
1727 "On this he put the bow down, letting it lean against the door,164 with"
1727 "the arrow standing against the tip of the bow. Then he took his seat"
1727 "again on the seat from which he had risen; and Antinous rebuked him"
1727 "saying:"
1727 ""
1727 "“Leiodes, what are you talking about? Your words are monstrous and"
1727 "intolerable; it makes me angry to listen to you. Shall, then, this bow"
1727 "take the life of many a chief among us, merely because you cannot bend"
1727 "it yourself? True, you were not born to be an archer, but there are"
1727 "others who will soon string it.”"
1727 ""
1727 "Then he said to Melanthius the goatherd, “Look sharp, light a fire in"
1727 "the court, and set a seat hard by with a sheep skin on it; bring us"
1727 "also a large ball of lard, from what they have in the house. Let us"
1727 "warm the bow and grease it—we will then make trial of it again, and"
1727 "bring the contest to an end.”"
1727 ""
1727 "Melanthius lit the fire, and set a seat covered with sheep skins beside"
1727 "it. He also brought a great ball of lard from what they had in the"
1727 "house, and the suitors warmed the bow and again made trial of it, but"
1727 "they were none of them nearly strong enough to string it. Nevertheless"

1727 ""
1727 "Then the swineherd and the stockman left the cloisters together, and"

1727 ""

1727 "Then Ulysses tore off his rags, and sprang on to the broad pavement"
1727 "with his bow and his quiver full of arrows. He shed the arrows on to"
1727 "the ground at his feet and said, “The mighty contest is at an end. I"
1727 "will now see whether Apollo will vouchsafe it to me to hit another mark"
1727 "which no man has yet hit.”"
1727 ""
1727 "On this he aimed a deadly arrow at Antinous, who was about to take up a"
1727 "two-handled gold cup to drink his wine and already had it in his hands."
1727 "He had no thought of death—who amongst all the revellers would think"
1727 "that one man, however brave, would stand alone among so many and kill"
1727 "him? The arrow struck Antinous in the throat, and the point went clean"
1727 "through his neck, so that he fell over and the cup dropped from his"
1727 "hand, while a thick stream of blood gushed from his nostrils. He kicked"
1727 "the table from him and upset the things on it, so that the bread and"
1727 "roasted meats were all soiled as they fell over on to the ground.166"
1727 "The suitors were in an uproar when they saw that a man had been hit;"
1727 "they sprang in dismay one and all of them from their seats and looked"
1727 "everywhere towards the walls, but there was neither shield nor spear,"
1727 "and they rebuked Ulysses very angrily. “Stranger,” said they, “you"
1727 "shall pay for shooting people in this way: you shall see no other"
1727 "contest; you are a doomed man; he whom you have slain was the foremost"

1727 "PENELOPE EVENTUALLY RECOGNISES HER HUSBAND—EARLY IN THE MORNING"
1727 "ULYSSES, TELEMACHUS, EUMAEUS, AND PHILOETIUS LEAVE THE TOWN."
1727 ""
1727 ""
1727 "Euryclea now went upstairs laughing to tell her mistress that her dear"
1727 "husband had come home. Her aged knees became young again and her feet"
1727 "were nimble for joy as she went up to her mistress and bent over her"
1727 "head to speak to her. “Wake up Penelope, my dear child,” she exclaimed,"
1727 "“and see with your own eyes something that you have been wanting this"
1727 "long time past. Ulysses has at last indeed come home again, and has"
1727 "killed the suitors who were giving so much trouble in his house, eating"
1727 "up his estate and ill treating his son.”"
1727 ""
1727 "“My good nurse,” answered Penelope, “you must be mad. The gods"
1727 "sometimes send some very sensible people out of their minds, and make"
1727 "foolish people become sensible. This is what they must have been doing"
1727 "to you; for you always used to be a reasonable person. Why should you"
1727 "thus mock me when I have trouble enough already—talking such nonsense,"
1727 "and waking me up out of a sweet sleep that had taken possession of my"
1727 "eyes and closed them? I have never slept so soundly from the day my"
1727 "poor husband went to that city with the ill-omened name. Go back again"
1727 "into the women’s room; if it had been any one else who had woke me up"
1727 "to bring me such absurd news I should have sent her away with a severe"
1727 "scolding. As it is your age shall protect you.”"
1727 ""

1727 "true as I tell you that Ulysses is come home again. He was the stranger"
1727 "whom they all kept on treating so badly in the cloister. Telemachus"
1727 "knew all the time that he was come back, but kept his father’s secret"
1727 "that he might have his revenge on all these wicked people.”"
1727 ""

1727 "fear that someone might come here and deceive me with a lying story;"
1727 "for there are many very wicked people going about. Jove’s daughter"
1727 "Helen would never have yielded herself to a man from a foreign country,"
1727 "if she had known that the sons of Achaeans would come after her and"

1727 ""

1727 ""
1727 "“My dear,” answered Ulysses, “why should you press me to tell you?"

1727 "THE GHOSTS OF THE SUITORS IN HADES—ULYSSES AND HIS MEN GO TO THE HOUSE"
1727 "OF LAERTES—THE PEOPLE OF ITHACA COME OUT TO ATTACK ULYSSES, BUT MINERVA"
1727 "CONCLUDES A PEACE."

1727 "Then Mercury of Cyllene summoned the ghosts of the suitors, and in his"
1727 "hand he held the fair golden wand with which he seals men’s eyes in"
1727 "sleep or wakes them just as he pleases; with this he roused the ghosts"
1727 "and led them, while they followed whining and gibbering behind him. As"
1727 "bats fly squealing in the hollow of some great cave, when one of them"
1727 "has fallen out of the cluster in which they hang, even so did the"
1727 "ghosts whine and squeal as Mercury the healer of sorrow led them down"
1727 "into the dark abode of death. When they had passed the waters of"
1727 "Oceanus and the rock Leucas, they came to the gates of the sun and the"
1727 "land of dreams, whereon they reached the meadow of asphodel where dwell"

1727 ""
1727 "Here they found the ghost of Achilles son of Peleus, with those of"
1727 "Patroclus, Antilochus, and Ajax, who was the finest and handsomest man"
1727 "of all the Danaans after the son of Peleus himself."

1727 "my country. Poor fellow, yet the omens were good for him when he left"
1727 "me. The birds all flew on our right hands, and both he and I rejoiced"
1727 "to see them as we parted, for we had every hope that we should have"
1727 "another friendly meeting and exchange presents.”"
1727 ""
1727 "A dark cloud of sorrow fell upon Laertes as he listened. He filled both"
1727 "hands with the dust from off the ground and poured it over his grey"
1727 "head, groaning heavily as he did so. The heart of Ulysses was touched,"
1727 "and his nostrils quivered as he looked upon his father; then he sprang"
1727 "towards him, flung his arms about him and kissed him, saying, “I am he,"
1727 "father, about whom you are asking—I have returned after having been"
1727 "away for twenty years. But cease your sighing and lamentation—we have"
1727 "no time to lose, for I should tell you that I have been killing the"
1727 "suitors in my house, to punish them for their insolence and crimes.”"
1727 ""
1727 "“If you really are my son Ulysses,” replied Laertes, “and have come"
1727 "back again, you must give me such manifest proof of your identity as"
1727 "shall convince me.”"
1727 ""
1727 "“First observe this scar,” answered Ulysses, “which I got from a boar’s"
1727 "tusk when I was hunting on Mt. Parnassus. You and my mother had sent me"
1727 "to Autolycus, my mother’s father, to receive the presents which when he"
1727 "was over here he had promised to give me. Furthermore I will point out"
1727 "to you the trees in the vineyard which you gave me, and I asked you all"
1727 "about them as I followed you round the garden. We went over them all,"
1727 "and you told me their names and what they all were. You gave me"
1727 "thirteen pear trees, ten apple trees, and forty fig trees; you also"
1727 "said you would give me fifty rows of vines; there was corn planted"
1727 "between each row, and they yield grapes of every kind when the heat of"
1727 "heaven has been laid heavy upon them.”"
1727 ""
1727 "Laertes’ strength failed him when he heard the convincing proofs which"
1727 "his son had given him. He threw his arms about him, and Ulysses had to"
1727 "support him, or he would have gone off into a swoon; but as soon as he"
1727 "came to, and was beginning to recover his senses, he said, “O father"
1727 "Jove, then you gods are still in Olympus after all, if the suitors have"
1727 "really been punished for their insolence and folly. Nevertheless, I am"
1727 "much afraid that I shall have all the townspeople of Ithaca up here"
1727 "directly, and they will be sending messengers everywhere throughout the"
1727 "cities of the Cephallenians.”"
1727 ""
1727 "Ulysses answered, “Take heart and do not trouble yourself about that,"
1727 "but let us go into the house hard by your garden. I have already told"
1727 "Telemachus, Philoetius, and Eumaeus to go on there and get dinner ready"
1727 "as soon as possible.”"
1727 ""
1727 "Thus conversing the two made their way towards the house. When they got"
1727 "there they found Telemachus with the stockman and the swineherd cutting"
1727 "up meat and mixing wine with water. Then the old Sicel woman took"
1727 "Laertes inside and washed him and anointed him with oil. She put him on"
1727 "a good cloak, and Minerva came up to him and gave him a more imposing"
1727 "presence, making him taller and stouter than before. When he came back"
1727 "his son was surprised to see him looking so like an immortal, and said"
1727 "to him, “My dear father, some one of the gods has been making you much"
1727 "taller and better-looking.”"
1727 ""
1727 "Laertes answered, “Would, by Father Jove, Minerva, and Apollo, that I"
1727 "were the man I was when I ruled among the Cephallenians, and took"
1727 "Nericum, that strong fortress on the foreland. If I were still what I"
1727 "then was and had been in our house yesterday with my armour on, I"
1727 "should have been able to stand by you and help you against the suitors."
1727 "I should have killed a great many of them, and you would have rejoiced"
1727 "to see it.”"
1727 ""
1727 "Thus did they converse; but the others, when they had finished their"
1727 "work and the feast was ready, left off working, and took each his"
1727 "proper place on the benches and seats. Then they began eating; by and"
1727 "by old Dolius and his sons left their work and came up, for their"
1727 "mother, the Sicel woman who looked after Laertes now that he was"
1727 "growing old, had been to fetch them. When they saw Ulysses and were"
1727 "certain it was he, they stood there lost in astonishment; but Ulysses"
1727 "scolded them good naturedly and said, “Sit down to your dinner, old"
1727 "man, and never mind about your surprise; we have been wanting to begin"
1727 "for some time and have been waiting for you.”"
1727 ""
1727 "Then Dolius put out both his hands and went up to Ulysses. “Sir,” said"
1727 "he, seizing his master’s hand and kissing it at the wrist, “we have"
1727 "long been wishing you home: and now heaven has restored you to us after"
1727 "we had given up hoping. All hail, therefore, and may the gods prosper"
1727 "you.187 But tell me, does Penelope already know of your return, or"
1727 "shall we send some one to tell her?”"
1727 ""
1727 "“Old man,” answered Ulysses, “she knows already, so you need not"
1727 "trouble about that.” On this he took his seat, and the sons of Dolius"
1727 "gathered round Ulysses to give him greeting and embrace him one after"
1727 "the other; then they took their seats in due order near Dolius their"
1727 "father."
1727 ""
1727 "While they were thus busy getting their dinner ready, Rumour went round"
1727 "the town, and noised abroad the terrible fate that had befallen the"
1727 "suitors; as soon, therefore, as the people heard of it they gathered"
1727 "from every quarter, groaning and hooting before the house of Ulysses."
1727 "They took the dead away, buried every man his own, and put the bodies"
1727 "of those who came from elsewhere on board the fishing vessels, for the"
1727 "fishermen to take each of them to his own place. They then met angrily"
1727 "in the place of assembly, and when they were got together Eupeithes"
1727 "rose to speak. He was overwhelmed with grief for the death of his son"
1727 "Antinous, who had been the first man killed by Ulysses, so he said,"
1727 "weeping bitterly, “My friends, this man has done the Achaeans great"
1727 "wrong. He took many of our best men away with him in his fleet, and he"
1727 "has lost both ships and men; now, moreover, on his return he has been"
1727 "killing all the foremost men among the Cephallenians. Let us be up and"
1727 "doing before he can get away to Pylos or to Elis where the Epeans rule,"
1727 "or we shall be ashamed of ourselves for ever afterwards. It will be an"
1727 "everlasting disgrace to us if we do not avenge the murder of our sons"
1727 "and brothers. For my own part I should have no more pleasure in life,"
1727 "but had rather die at once. Let us be up, then, and after them, before"
1727 "they can cross over to the main land.”"
1727 ""
1727 "He wept as he spoke and every one pitied him. But Medon and the bard"
1727 "Phemius had now woke up, and came to them from the house of Ulysses."
1727 "Every one was astonished at seeing them, but they stood in the middle"

1727 ""
1727 "On this pale fear laid hold of them, and old Halitherses, son of"
1727 "Mastor, rose to speak, for he was the only man among them who knew both"
1727 "past and future; so he spoke to them plainly and in all honesty,"
1727 "saying,"
1727 ""

1727 ""

1727 "Then Minerva said to Jove, “Father, son of Saturn, king of kings,"

1727 ""

1727 ""

1727 ""

1727 "[31] [ The Greek is {Greek} cf. “Iliad” ii. 408 {Greek} Surely the"
1727 "{Greek} of the Odyssean passage was due to the {Greek} of the “Iliad.”"
1727 "No other reason suggests itself for the making Menelaus return on the"
1727 "very day of the feast given by Orestes. The fact that in the “Iliad”"
1727 "Menelaus came to a banquet without waiting for an invitation,"
1727 "determines the writer of the “Odyssey” to make him come to a banquet,"
1727 "also uninvited, but as circumstances did not permit of his having been"
1727 "invited, his coming uninvited is shown to have been due to chance. I do"

1727 "strangeness of the coincidence to unconscious cerebration and"

1727 "earlier buildings would have no arches, and would, one would suppose,"
1727 "be mainly timber. The Odyssean {Greek} were the sheds that ran round"
1727 "the yard as the arches do now. The {Greek} was the one through which"
1727 "the main entrance passed, and which was hence “noisy,” or"
1727 "reverberating. It had an upper story in which visitors were often"
1727 "lodged.]"

1727 "[35] [ This journey is an impossible one. Telemachus and Pisistratus"
1727 "would have been obliged to drive over the Taygetus range, over which"
1727 "there has never yet been a road for wheeled vehicles. It is plain"
1727 "therefore that the audience for whom the “Odyssey” was written was one"
1727 "that would be unlikely to know anything about the topography of the"
1727 "Peloponnese, so that the writer might take what liberties she chose.]"

1727 "[36] [ The lines which I have enclosed in brackets are evidently an"
1727 "afterthought—added probably by the writer herself—for they evince the"
1727 "same instinctively greater interest in anything that may concern a"
1727 "woman, which is so noticeable throughout the poem. There is no further"
1727 "sign of any special festivities nor of any other guests than Telemachus"
1727 "and Pisistratus, until lines 621-624 (ordinarily enclosed in brackets)"
1727 "are abruptly introduced, probably with a view of trying to carry off"
1727 "the introduction of the lines now in question."
1727 ""
1727 "The addition was, I imagine, suggested by a desire to excuse and"
1727 "explain the non-appearance of Hermione in bk. xv., as also of both"
1727 "Hermione and Megapenthes in the rest of bk. iv. Megapenthes in bk. xv."
1727 "seems to be still a bachelor: the presumption therefore is that bk. xv."
1727 "was written before the story of his marriage here given. I take it he"
1727 "is only married here because his sister is being married. She having"
1727 "been properly attended to, Megapenthes might as well be married at the"
1727 "same time. Hermione could not now be less than thirty."
1727 ""

1727 "the Odyssey”, p. 136-138. See also p. 256 of the same book.]"

1727 "[48] [ I give the usual translation, but I do not believe the Greek"
1727 "will warrant it. The Greek reads {Greek}."
1727 ""
1727 "This is usually held to mean that Ithaca is an island fit for breeding"
1727 "goats, and on that account more delectable to the speaker than it would"
1727 "have been if it were fit for breeding horses. I find little authority"
1727 "for such a translation; the most equitable translation of the text as"
1727 "it stands is, “Ithaca is an island fit for breeding goats, and"
1727 "delectable rather than fit for breeding horses; for not one of the"
1727 "islands is good driving ground, nor well meadowed.” Surely the writer"
1727 "does not mean that a pleasant or delectable island would not be fit for"
1727 "breeding horses? The most equitable translation, therefore, of the"
1727 "present text being thus halt and impotent, we may suspect corruption,"
1727 "and I hazard the following emendation, though I have not adopted it in"
1727 "my translation, as fearing that it would be deemed too fanciful. I"
1727 "would read:—{Greek}."
1727 ""
1727 "As far as scanning goes the {Greek} is not necessary; {Greek} iv. 72,"
1727 "(Footnote Greek) iv. 233, to go no further afield than earlier lines of"
1727 "the same book, give sufficient authority for {Greek}, but the {Greek}"
1727 "would not be redundant; it would emphasise the surprise of the"
1727 "contrast, and I should prefer to have it, though it is not very"
1727 "important either way. This reading of course should be translated"
1727 "“Ithaca is an island fit for breeding goats, and (by your leave) itself"
1727 "a horseman rather than fit for breeding horses—for not one of the"
1727 "islands is good and well meadowed ground.”"
1727 ""
1727 "This would be sure to baffle the Alexandrian editors. “How,” they would"
1727 "ask themselves, “could an island be a horseman?” and they would cast"
1727 "about for an emendation. A visit to the top of Mt. Eryx might perhaps"
1727 "make the meaning intelligible, and suggest my proposed restoration of"
1727 "the text to the reader as readily as it did to myself."
1727 ""
1727 "I have elsewhere stated my conviction that the writer of the “Odyssey”"
1727 "was familiar with the old Sican city at the top of Mt. Eryx, and that"
1727 "the Aegadean islands which are so striking when seen thence did duty"
1727 "with her for the Ionian islands—Marettimo, the highest and most"
1727 "westerly of the group, standing for Ithaca. When seen from the top of"
1727 "Mt. Eryx Marettimo shows as it should do according to “Od.” ix. 25, 26,"
1727 "“on the horizon, all highest up in the sea towards the West,” while the"
1727 "other islands lie “some way off it to the East.” As we descend to"
1727 "Trapani, Marettimo appears to sink on to the top of the island of"
1727 "Levanzo, behind which it disappears. My friend, the late Signor E."
1727 "Biaggini, pointed to it once as it was just standing on the top of"
1727 "Levanzo, and said to me “Come cavalca bene” (“How well it rides”), and"
1727 "this immediately suggested my emendation to me. Later on I found in the"
1727 "hymn to the Pythian Apollo (which abounds with tags taken from the"
1727 "“Odyssey”) a line ending {Greek} which strengthened my suspicion that"
1727 "this was the original ending of the second of the two lines above under"
1727 "consideration.]"

1727 "[81] [ This line is enclosed in brackets in the received text, and is"
1727 "omitted (with note) by Messrs. Butcher & Lang. But lines enclosed in"
1727 "brackets are almost always genuine; all that brackets mean is that the"
1727 "bracketed passage puzzled some early editor, who nevertheless found it"
1727 "too well established in the text to venture on omitting it. In the"
1727 "present case the line bracketed is the very last which a full-grown"
1727 "male editor would be likely to interpolate. It is safer to infer that"
1727 "the writer, a young woman, not knowing or caring at which end of the"
1727 "ship the rudder should be, determined to make sure by placing it at"
1727 "both ends, which we shall find she presently does by repeating it (line"
1727 "340) at the stern of the ship. As for the two rocks thrown, the first I"
1727 "take to be the Asinelli, see map facing p. 80. The second I see as the"
1727 "two contiguous islands of the Formiche, which are treated as one, see"
1727 "map facing p. 108. The Asinelli is an island shaped like a boat, and"
1727 "pointing to the island of Favognana. I think the authoress’s"
1727 "compatriots, who probably did not like her much better that she did"
1727 "them, jeered at the absurdity of Ulysses’ conduct, and saw the Asinelli"
1727 "or “donkeys,” not as the rock thrown by Polyphemus, but as the boat"
1727 "itself containing Ulysses and his men.]"
1727 ""

1727 "[99] [ Off the coast of Sicily and South Italy, in the month of May, I"
1727 "have seen men fastened half way up a boat’s mast with their feet"
1727 "resting on a crosspiece, just large enough to support them. From this"
1727 "point of vantage they spear sword-fish. When I saw men thus employed I"
1727 "could hardly doubt that the writer of the “Odyssey” had seen others"
1727 "like them, and had them in her mind when describing the binding of"
1727 "Ulysses. I have therefore with some diffidence ventured to depart from"
1727 "the received translation of ἰστοπέδη (cf. Alcaeus frag. 18, where,"
1727 "however, it is very hard to say what ἰστοπέδαν means). In Sophocles’"
1727 "Lexicon I find a reference to Chrysostom (l, 242, A. Ed. Benedictine"
1727 "Paris 1834-1839) for the word ἰστοπόδη, which is probably the same as"
1727 "ἰστοπέδη, but I have looked for the passage in vain.]"

1727 "[100] [ The writer is at fault here and tries to put it off on Circe."
1727 "When Ulysses comes to take the route prescribed by Circe, he ought to"
1727 "pass either the Wanderers or some other difficulty of which we are not"
1727 "told, but he does not do so. The Planctae, or Wanderers, merge into"
1727 "Scylla and Charybdis, and the alternative between them and something"
1727 "untold merges into the alternative whether Ulysses had better choose"
1727 "Scylla or Charybdis. Yet from line 260, it seems we are to consider the"
1727 "Wanderers as having been passed by Ulysses; this appears even more"
1727 "plainly from xxiii. 327, in which Ulysses expressly mentions the"
1727 "Wandering rocks as having been between the Sirens and Scylla and"
1727 "Charybdis. The writer, however, is evidently unaware that she does not"
1727 "quite understand her own story; her difficulty was perhaps due to the"
1727 "fact that though Trapanese sailors had given her a fair idea as to"
1727 "where all her other localities really were, no one in those days more"
1727 "than in our own could localise the Planctae, which in fact, as Buttmann"
1727 "has argued, were derived not from any particular spot, but from"
1727 "sailors’ tales about the difficulties of navigating the group of the"
1727 "Aeolian islands as a whole (see note on “Od.” x. 3). Still the matter"
1727 "of the poor doves caught her fancy, so she would not forgo them. The"
1727 "whirlwinds of fire and the smoke that hangs on Scylla suggests allusion"
1727 "to Stromboli and perhaps even Etna. Scylla is on the Italian side, and"
1727 "therefore may be said to look West. It is about 8 miles thence to the"
1727 "Sicilian coast, so Ulysses may be perfectly well told that after"
1727 "passing Scylla he will come to the Thrinacian island or Sicily."
1727 "Charybdis is transposed to a site some few miles to the north of its"
1727 "actual position.]"
1727 ""

1727 "attack Trapani, but the “Madonna di Trapani” crushed them under this"
1727 "rock just as they were coming into port. My friend Cavaliere"
1727 "Giannitrapani of Trapani told me that his father used to tell him when"
1727 "he was a boy that if he would drop exactly three drops of oil on to the"
1727 "water near the rock, he would see the ship still at the bottom. The"
1727 "legend is evidently a Christianised version of the Odyssean story,"
1727 "while the name supplies the additional detail that the disaster"

1727 "[156] [ Lower down (line 143) Euryclea says it was herself that had"

1727 "fluctuating between Euryclea and Eurynome as the name for the old"
1727 "nurse. She probably originally meant to call her Euryclea, but finding"
1727 "it not immediately easy to make Euryclea scan in xvii. 495, she hastily"
1727 "called her Eurynome, intending either to alter this name later or to"
1727 "change the earlier Euryclea’s into Eurynome. She then drifted in to"
1727 "Eurynome as convenience further directed, still nevertheless hankering"
1727 "after Euryclea, till at last she found that the path of least"
1727 "resistance would lie in the direction of making Eurynome and Euryclea"
1727 "two persons. Therefore in xxiii. 289-292 both Eurynome and “the nurse”"
1727 "(who can be none other than Euryclea) come on together. I do not say"
1727 "that this is feminine, but it is not unfeminine.]"

1727 "Surely the difficulty of this passage has been overrated. I suppose the"
1727 "iron part of the axe to have been wedged into the handle, or bound"
1727 "securely to it—the handle being half buried in the ground. The axe"
1727 "would be placed edgeways towards the archer, and he would have to shoot"
1727 "his arrow through the hole into which the handle was fitted when the"
1727 "axe was in use. Twelve axes were placed in a row all at the same"
1727 "height, all exactly in front of one another, all edgeways to Ulysses"

1727 "cannot see how the Greek can bear any other interpretation, the words"
1727 "being, {Greek}"

1727 "From the top of the tower the outer world was to be told what was going"
1727 "on, but people could not get in by the ὀρσοθύρα: they would have to"
1727 "come in by the main entrance, and Melanthius explains that the mouth of"
1727 "the narrow passage (which was in the lands of Ulysses and his friends)"
1727 "commanded the only entrance by which help could come, so that there"
1727 "would be nothing gained by raising an alarm."
1727 "    As for the ῥῶγες of line 143, no commentator ancient or modern has"
1727 "    been able to say what was intended—but whatever they were,"
1727 "    Melanthius could never carry twelve shields, twelve helmets, and"
1727 "    twelve spears. Moreover, where he could go the others could go"
1727 "    also. If a dozen suitors had followed Melanthius into the house"
1727 "    they could have attacked Ulysses in the rear, in which case, unless"
1727 "    Minerva had intervened promptly, the “Odyssey” would have had a"
1727 "    different ending. But throughout the scene we are in a region of"
1727 "    extravagance rather than of true fiction—it cannot be taken"
1727 "    seriously by any but the very serious, until we come to the episode"
1727 "    of Phemius and Medon, where the writer begins to be at home again.]"
